GLOSSARY
Aman        -      Rice planted before or during the monsoon and harvested in
November or December.
Aus         -      Rice planted during February or March and harvested during
June or July.
B.          -     When preceding a crop means broadcast.
Beel        -     Low-lying area subject to flooding by rain or river water.
Boro        -     Rice transplanted in January or Februarary and harvested in
May or June.
District    -     Administrative unit comprising a number of Upazilas. in the
charge of a Deputy Commissioner
Khal        -     Natural channel.
Khalasi     -      Staff employed by BWDB to inspect embankments or operate
sluices.
Kharif      -      Summer cropping season (May through November).
Monsoon     -      Period of rains starting in June and ending in October.
Rabi        -     Winter cropping season (November through May).
T.          -     When preceding a crop means transplanted.
Upazila     -      Smallest administrative unit of local government.

Government of the Netherlands (GON), the European Economic Community (EC) and
the World Food Programme (WFP). Mission members for IDA were Messrs. H. van
Voorthuizen, G. Schokman, J. Bredie, J. Pruntel (IDA), C. Amorin, A. Hamid, T.
Jansen and H.R. Khan (Consultants). Mr. J. van der Laan participated for GON.
The EC was represented by Messrs. J. Jonckers, N. Joncheere (EC), Q. Chowdhury
and R. van der Val (Consultants). Messrs. J. Conway of WFP and A. Filotti,
Chief Technical Adviser for PFW, also participated in the mission.

BANGLADESH
BWDB SYSTEMS REHABILITATION PROJECT
Credit and Proiect Sunmmary
Borrower:                People's Republic of Bangladesh
Amount:                  SDR40.8 million (US$53.9 million equivalent)
Terms:                   Standard IDA Terms with a maturity of 40 years
Cofinanciers:            The European Economic Ccmmunity (EC) will provide a
grant of ECU 13.5 million (US$15.0 million equivalent)
and the Government of tSe Netherlands (GON) a grant of
DFI 25.23 million (US$12.6 million equivalent)
Proiect Description:    The main objectives of the project are to protect and
increase agricultural production and incomes and to
raise standards of living, particularly of landless
people and women, through rehabilitation and improved
operation and maintenance (O&M) of BWDB's existing
flood control. drainage. and irrigation projects.
Subsidiary objectives are to (a) increase financing
and encourage more efficient use of funds for O&M
through better planning and reduction of costs, where
possible, (b) improve skills and motivation of BWDB
staff for O&M tasks, and (c) increase involvement of
the farmers in planning, construction, operation and
maintenance, particularly of on-farm development
works. These objectives would be achieved through (a)
rehabilitation and improvement of about 80
subprojects, covering a gross area of about 600,000 ha
which have deteriorated due to inadequate maintenance,
(b) introduction of improved O&H in BWDB's projects,
starting in four schemes where infrastructure still is
in reasonably good condition, but not maintained
properly, (c) on-farm development works and improved
O&M in two subprojects with farmers' participation,
(d) training of BWDB staff and beneficiaries,
particularly for O&M, and (e) provision of technical
assistance to assist BWDB in planning, design and
execution of rehabilitation, improvement, on-farm
development and O&M works and to help strengthen its
organization for O&M and staff development. The World
Food Programme (WFP) would provide food to finance
part of construction and maintenance of earthworks to
be carried out under the rehabilitation and improved
O&M components.



Benefits and Risks'     As * result of the project, the average incomes of
some 700,000 families would  acreage by about 25Z due
to higher crop production resulting 'rou improved
drainage conditions and expansion of irrigation. At
the same time, their current incomes would be better
protected against losses due to deterioration of
BVDB's facilities. The project would also generate an
additional labor demand of about 25,000 man-years per
year for farm activities. Additional employment
created during the rehabilisation and OxM activities
is estimated at about 100,000 man-years; this would
mainly benefit the landless. Possible delays in pre-
construction activities may delay project
implementation.   However, land acquisition
requirements for this project are minimal and
arrangements have been incorporated in the project
design to minimize the risk of delays. Shortage of
local funds for rehabilitation and O&M may also delay
implementation. The contribution of GOB has there'ore
been kept at a minimum and external donors would
finance a substantial part of the incremental OEM
costs of the subprojects.  Special emphasis would also
be given in the project to reducing the cost and
improving the standards of OEM.

BANGLADESH
BWDB SYSTEMS REHABILITATION PROJECT
I. SECTOR AND PROJECT BACKGROUND
A. The Agriculture Sector
Setting and Recent Trends
1.01       Bangladesh has a population of about 110 million, which continues
to grow at an annual rate of about 2.4Z. The large majority of the people
live in the rural areas and depend on agriculture for a living. Population
density now exceeds 1,000 persons per square km of cultivable land and more
than 70? of the people are living in conditions of absolute poverty. Per
capita income is US$170. Developing a capability for self-sufficiency in food
grains has been the central objective of the Government of Bangladesh's (GOB)
strategy for agricultural development since the country became independent in
1971. During the 1980s GOB has endeavored to support the development of the
sector by (a) reorientation of public expenditures in favor of low-cost
irrigation, flood control, and drainage projects, (b) economic libera;izai"on
to encourage private sector participation in the distribution of agricultural
inputs, and (c) strengthening of agricultural support services such as
extension, research, credit and farmers' organizations. However,
suscept.b"lity to weather vagaries (floods and droughts) and inadequate
investments in fluod control and irrigation have resulted in lower than
expected growth rates. Production of food grains still falls 102-15? short of
requirements and imports vary between one and two million m ton in most years.
1.02       The agricultural sector contributes at present about one half of
GDP, employs about 60? of the labor force and generates about 60? of the
country's export earnings. Agriculture value added increased at an annual
rate of about 32 over the period FY81-86. However, during the last three
years (FY87-89), the growth rate averaged only about 1.7? due to a slow down
in minor irrigation development, the exceptional floods of 1987 and 1988, and
a snarp cutback in development expenditures for agriculture and water
resources. Production in the crop subsector continues to be dominated by food
grains, accounting for about 85? of the cultivated area. Food grain
production increased at an average rate of 2.82 per year during the last
decade, which is about 0.3? higher than the inctzase in population during the
same period. About two thirds of this increase came from dry season cropping,
confirming the critical role of irrigation, together with fertilizers and
better seeds, in helping the country attain food self-sufficiency. Reliable
irrigation typically increases production per unit of land by about 30? during
the monsoon season and at least doubles it during the dry season. Irrigation
has been extended over the past 15 years by about 100,000 ha per year, mainly
through the installation of hand tubewells (HTW), shallow tubewell.; (STW) and



deep tubewells (DTW) for the extraction of groundwater, and use of low lift
pumps (LLP) to exploit surface water resources. In addition, the construction
of flood control and drainage works has helped to secure harvesting of dry
season crops before the onset of the monsoon ra.j.u and has made it possible to
introduce improved technology, including supplementary irrigation, to raise
the production of rainfed wet season crops. To som extent, the growth in
food grain production has been achieved at the expense of other crops. Except
for potatoes and onions, the areas under other food crops have declined. Jute
production has declined at an average annual rate of 1.4Z despite an average
yield increase of 1.3Z. On the other hand, tea production has increased at a
rate of 2.71 pe: annum, mainly as a result of improved yields, while cagar
production has xncreased by about 1.31 per year as a result of area expansion.
Growth rates during the last decade in the 14vestock, fisheries, and forestry
sub-sectors were mixed. The increase in production of milk was lower, that of
meat about equal, and that of eggs, fish and fuel wood slightly higher than
the growth in population during the period.
Potential for Growth
1.03       Irrigation has the potential to continue to lead agricultural
growth in the future. The 1986 draft National Water Plan estimates that the
present irrigation coverage of about 2.3 million ha could more than double
using relatively inexpensive minor irrigation equipment to exploit readily
available groundwater and surface water resources. In the long term, the
water resources of the Brabmaputra, Ganges and Meghna rivers would have to be
developed to expand irrigation further. Developing the irrigation potential
in the near and medium term will mainly depend on initiatives by the private
sector. Public sectfr support should be limited to providing a policy
environment which stimulates sales of agricultural inputs and equipment,
improvisg the quality of agricultural support serv-Aces, and providing
essential infrastructure such as basic flood control and irrigation
infrastructure. Flood control and drainage facilities, now covering about 2.5
million ha, should be expanded to cover all areas (about five million he)
where such facilities would help limit pre-monsoon flood damage to standing
crops and intensify the production of monsoon season crops. A good potential
also exists for further growth in the fisheries sub-sector through development
of flood plain fisheries, pond aquaculture and coastal aquaculture, especially
shrimp production. Prospects for growth in the livestock subsector are
limited, mainly because of the scarcity of land for grazing and fodder
production.
Prerequisitas for Growth
1.04       In order to stimulate agricultural production and further develop
the country's water resources, GOB should (a) further liberalize the sale of
minor irrigation equipment, (b) reorient and accelerate implementation of the
flood control and drainage investment program to support further minor
irrigation and fisheries development, (c) improve and reorient the
productivity of the agricultural research and extension systems to meet the
needs for intensification and diversification of production, and (d)
strengthen the agriceltural credit system to allow access to it by a majority



of producers.  Equally important, GOB should ensure that tt'e existing pu:'Aic
stock of flood control, drainage, and irrigation facilities is efficiently
operated, adequately maintained, and fully utilized with maximum participation
of the beneficiaries. Finally, special care should be taken to allow
disadvantaged groups such as landless people and women, to contribute to and
share in the benefits of growth in the agricultural sector.
Past Investments in the Water Resources Sector
1.05       The Bank's 1989 Public Expenditure Review confirmed that many parts
of GOB's recurrent budget continue to be seriously under-funded, particularly
the operation and maintenance (O&M) of existing infrastructure. 'While
scarcity of local funds is an important factor, a lack of emphasis on O&M in
the planning process (as compared to new investments) has also proved to be a
major constraint. It is estimated that GOB's allocation under the Revenue
Budget for O&M of BWDB's flood control, drainage, and irrigation projects has
fallen some 302 - 402 short of requirements in recent years (para 1.16). At
the same time, cost recovery from the beneficiaries has been negligib'e (paras
4.09 through 4.14). As a result, all projects suffer from a lack of regular
maintenance and many have deteriorated to a stage where major rehabilitation
works have become necessary. Lack of regular maintenance not only reduces the
effectiveness of projects and curtails their potential benefits, it also
increases the risk of major flood damage, particularly during severe floods
such as those that occurred in 1987 and 1988. For want of better alternatives
GOB has attempted to address this situation on an ad-hoc basis by including
rehabilitation components in new projects, where possisle, or by expanding
emergency rehabilitation schemes following exceptional floods. The Food-for-
Work Programme (FFN) supported by the World Food Programme (WFP), has been a
major source of assistance over the years for undertaking repair works in
water sector projects. Each year, some 100,000 m ton of wheat are made
available under this program to resection embankments and re-excavate
irrigation and drainage channels.
1.06       There are other factors which limit the effectiveness of past
investments in water resources infrastructure. These include weaknesses in
the procedures and practices for O&M, and insufficient involvement of the
beneficiaries. Poor procedures for O&M are largely due to lack of planning,
insufficient staff capabilities and motivation, and a tendency of the
responsible agencies to favor the construction of new projects over the
maintenance of existing ones. Thir, in turn, reflects the unsatisfactory
funding for OWM. Little effort is being made to involve the beneficiaries on
a routine basis in the O&M of project facilities, except during emergencies
when farmers mobilize in large numbers to protect the embankments. Farmers
commonly hold the GOB agencies, which first arrange for construction of the
projects, responsible for OEM of the facilities. Changing this attitude is a
major challenge for GOB and will require (a) much closer consultation between
officials and beneficiaries to ensure that the plans and designs for the
schemes address all of their interests, (b) public education to stress the
need for farmers to assume some of the responsibilities and costs of
maintaining the facilities from which they benefit, and (c) enforcement of
legislation governing the payment of water charges by the beneficiaries.



-4-
Institutions Involved
1.07       In order to promote agricultural growth, Bangladesh must improve
the performance of its public sector institutions. These include the
Ministries of Agriculture and Forests (MOA), Fisheries and Livestock (MOF),
Irrigation, Water Development and Flood Control (MOI). Local Government, Rural
Development and Cooperatives (MLG) and the semi-autonomous agencies under
their control. The Bangladesh Water Development Board (BWDB), under MOI, is
responsible for planning, implementation and O&M of surface water irrigation,
flood crntrol, and drainage projects, including town protection and river
training schemes (see Section B of this Chapter). The Bangladesh Agricultural
Development Corporation (BADC), under MOA, is involved in development of minor
irrigation systems involving the use of LLPs as well as HTWs, STWs, and DTWs
to tap the aquifer. In the past, BADC also was the main supplier of farm
inputs, including fertilizer, seeds, and minor irrigation equipment. However,
in recent years some of these responsibilities, particularly at the retail
level, have been transferred to the private sector. The Bangladesh
Agricultural Rese&rch Council (BARC), also under MOA, coordinates the research
activities of specialized institutions covering rice, jute, sugar, forestry,
fishery, tea, livestock, and other crops and horticulture. The Department of
Agricultural Extension (DAE) of MOA provides extension services to the
farmers. The Bangladesh Rural Development Board (BRDB), under MLG, promotes
the growth of farmer cooperatives, which collect thrift deposits, promote
group management of minor irrigation equipment, and provide credit and inputs
to small farMers. The Master Plan Organization (MPO), under MOI, is
responsible for preparation and updating of a National Water Plan (NWP) for
the most effective long term use of the country's water resources.
1.08       In recent years the administrative responsibility for providing
services to the agricultural sector has been transferred to local government
institutions at the Zila and Upazila levels. Various programs to improve the
services are underway or due to start shortly. Agricultural research,
extension, and credit services, are being strengthened under national
programs, with external assistance by IDA and other donors. These services
are essential to securing the full benefits of investments in water resources.
IDA's Involvement in Agricultural Development
1.09       IDA lending to Bangladesh has emphasized the agricultural sector,
in recognition of the country's needs and priorities. Within the sector
particular emphasis has been placed on developing water resources,
strengthening agricultural services, and developing complementary
infrastructure in other sectors such as transport and power. Consistent with
GOB's priorities, increased production of food grains has been the main target
of IDA's lending in agriculture. Ongoing IDA-supported projects in the
agricultural sector includet Extension and Research II (Cr. 1215-BD), Deep
Tubewells II (Cr. 1287-BD), Rural Development II (Cr. 1384-BD), Agricultural
Research II (Cr. 1455-BD), BWDB Small Schemes (Cr. 1467-BD), Flood Control and
Drainage III and IV (Cr. 1591 and 1784-BD, respectively), and Small Scale
Flood Control, Drainage and Irrigation II (Cr. 1870-BD). Damage repair works



- 5 -
on water sector infrastructure following the floods of 1987 and 1988 are being
financed by IDA under Flood Rehabilitation II and III (Cr. 1876 and 2048-BD,
respectively) and under Credit 1870-BD.
1.10       Over the years, IDA and GOB staff have participated in a number
of joint studies. Of special significance was the Land and Water Resources
Survey (1972) that led GOB to change its strategy for developing water
resources to low-cost irrigation, flood control, and drainage schemes. Other
important studies are the Minor Irrigation Sector Review (1982) and the Review
of Agricultural Credit (1984). These studies supported agricultural policy
reforms, including the privatization of procurement and distribution of
irrigation equipment, and the improvement of farmers' access to institutional
credit. Reviews by IDA staff of the crop production and fisheries subsectors
have been completed in most respects. In addition, the Bank is the Executing
Agency for the UNDP-financed National Water Plan Project which entered its
second phase in 1988. IDA staff also have been involved in several studies to
improve the performance of BWDB (para 1.21). Finally, the Bank is leading an
international effort aimed at designing an Action Plan to control annual
flooding in Bangladesh.
B. Bangladesh Water Development Board
Mandate and Sources of Financing
1.11       BWDB was established in 1972, when the former East Pakistan Water
and Power Development Authority (EPWAPDA) was divided into two separate
agencies -- the BWDB and the Power Development Board (PDB). Under the
administrative control of !iOI, BWI)B's mandate is to plan, construct, operate
and maintain projects for surface water irrigation, flood control, and
drainage, including town protection and river training, throughout Bangladesh.
According to GOB's Third Five Year Plan (1985-90), BIWDB was expected to
increase the area under surface irrigation from about 290,000 ha to about
600,000 ha and the area with flood control and drainage facilities from about
2.5 million ha to about 3.3 million ha. However, progress has been slow and
it is unlikely that these targets will be achieved. BWDB is also responsible
for operating and maintaining about 400 completed projects ranging in size
from small single-structure schemes to large multi-purpose flood control,
drainage, and irrigation projects. The main sources of financing in FY89 were
(a) GOB's Development Budget with an allocation of about US$ 177 million for
the construction of about 40 new projects and rehabilitation of a large number
of existing schemes which were damaged during the floods of 192? and 1988, (b)
the Revenue Budget with an allocation of about US$ 24 million for O&M of
completed projects (including about US$ 9 million for debt service, taxes and
arrears), and (c) the Food-for-Work Programme (FFW) with an allocation of
97,500 m ton of wheat (valued at about US$ 16 million) of which about 902 is
utilized for the repair and maintenance of existing projects, and the balance
for the construction of new schemes. Since 1972 IDA has supported BWDB's
program with 14 credits, totalling US$386 million for 11 new projects und
three flood rehabilitation operations. Seven of the new projects and one of
the flood rehabilitation operations have been completed.



Organization and Staffing
1.12       BWDB's organization and staffing pattern reflect broadly the
division between planning, design, and construction of new schemes on the one
hand, and O&M of existing schemes on the other.  The organization  X managed
by a six member Board comprising a Chairman, and five Members for lanning,
Implementation, Operation and Maintenance, Administration, and Finance,
respectively. The headquarters organization, located in Dhaka, is primarily
responsible for administration, finance, planning and design while
construction and O&M are the responsibility of the field organization. As of
December 1987, BWDB employed 18,013 staff. About 1,575 were class I managers,
engineers, and administrators, and about 16,440 were class II, III and IV
middle and lower-level technical and support staff. About one third of BWDB
employees are assigned to O&M. However, these staff are also responsible for
construction and rehabilitation of small projects.
Past Performance and Current Problems
1.13        General.   BWDB suffers from a number of basic problems that are
common to most GOB organizations and are only partly within their control.
These include (a) poor motivation of staff to perform well, amongst others
because of inadequate incentives, (b) slow and complicated administrative
approval procedures and insufficient delegation of responsibilities at all
levels, and (c) inadequate budgets and slow release of funds. These problems
are reflected in BWDB's perfo.mance, both in planning and construction of new
projects and O&M of existing schemes.
1.14       Construction of New Proiects.  Project Performance Audit Reports
(PPAR) prepared for five completed IDA-financed projects indicate that four
have been successfull while one project (the Barisal Irrigation Project) has
not reached its full potential.1 However, the audit reports also indicate
that all completed projects experienced serious construction delays. On the
average it took about eight years to complete the schemes instead of five
years as originally planned. Projects currently under construction experience
similar delays, most of which occur at the pre-construction stage. There are
at least three sources for the delays.   First, BWDB does not have the in-
house capacity to conduct full feasibility studies for its new projects and
has to rely on consultants for this work. The administrative procedures for
appointing consultants are time-consuming and MOI must approve all
appointments. Second, BWDB's capacity to design project works is limited
because it has not been able to retain the required numbers of qualified
1 (a) Chandpur Irrigation Project, Cr. 340-BD, PPAR Report No. 3436, dated
April 30, 1981.
(b) Karnafuli Irrigation Project, Cr. 605-BD, and Barisal Irrigation
Project Cr. 542-BD, PPAR Report No. 6521, dated November 21, 1986.
(c) Muhuri Irrigation Project, Cr. 725-BD, PCR, dated March 10, 1989.
(d) Drainage and Flood Control Project, Cr. 864-BD, PCR, dated January,
13, 1989.



- 7 -
design engineers on its staff. Consequently, an increasing portion of the
design work also has to be contracted out to consultants, but BWDB then is
slow in approving the designs. Third, procedures for acquiring land for
construction are long and complicated and actual acquisiion of land often is
delayed because of a shortage of local funds. Construction of three IDA-
finarced flood control and drainage projects is at present seriously delayed
because of acute local funds shortfalls for land acquisition. Delays also
occur during the construction stage, due to procurement problems, insufficient
budget allocations, or slow release of funds. During FY87 and FY88, for
example, BWDB's actual expenditures fell about 20Z short of its original
allocations under the Development Budget. Poor qualLty of construction due to
inadequate supervision of works by BWDB staff was a major problem in the past.
In recent years BWDB has employed consultants on several projects to monitor
construction works and help maintain quality standards. Quality of works has
indeed improved but there still remains substantial room for further
improvement.
1.15       Operation and Maintenance of Existing Projects.  The potential
benefits of past investments in BWDB's projects have not been fully realized
due to weaknesses in operation and maintenance resulting in deterioration of
the systems. The underlying reasons for these weaknesses include (a)
inadequate budget allocations, (b) inefficient use of available funds, (c)
deficiencies in BWDB's organizational arrangements, procedures and staff
capabilities for O&K, and (d) insufficient participation of farmers.
1.16       As mentioned in para 1.05, allocations under GOB's Revenue Budget
for O&M of BWDB's projects have consistently fallen short of requirements in
recent years. For example, the total requirement in FY89 for O&M of about 400
projects was estimated at about US$40 million equivalent. However, the
allocation for O&M activities under the Revenue Budget was only about US$15
million equivalent while another US$15 million worth of works was undertaken
under FFW.  The shortfall in funding was therefore about US$10 million
equivalent. This corresponds to about 25Z of BWDB's total requirement for OEM
and about 40Z of funding needed under the Revenue Budget   Since BWDB gives
priority to the payment of staff salaries and electricity bills, the shortfall
in funding is particularly severe for maintenance works. It is estimated that
BWDB's budget requirements for OEM increase by about 5Z per year on account of
new projects being completed. A sustained annual increase of 102 (in real
terms) of BWDB's Revenue Budget over a period of about ten years would be
needed to close the financing gap (see Annex 6 for further details on OEM cost
estimates and budget allocations).
1.17       While the budget provisions for O&M are too low, the use of funds
allocated is less th&n optimal. The cost of establishment accounted in FY89
for about 35? of BWDB's estimated requirements for OEM (total of Revenue
Budget and FFW). This is much higher than one normally would expect and
reflects the presence of a substantial staff surplus in BWDB. For all BWDB
operations corAbined, i.e. planning and construction of new works and OEM of
existing projects, staff pay and allowances accounted for 17Z of expenditures
in FY88, other establishment costs for 4? and works for 79?. Normally, when
operations are conducted efficiently, the establishment cost should not exceed



- 8 -
10? - 12Z of total expenditures. A detailed analysis of O&M activities and
staff requirements in three major irrigation projects was undertaken during
the preparation of the project. The analysis confirmed that BIWDB's present
staff in the three projects exceeds requirements by a large margin.
1.18       As increasing numbers of projects are being completed, BWDB's O&M
function is rapidly gaining importance. This has as yet not been reflected
adequately in its organizational set-up and staff skills. Ideally, the
construction of new and O&M of existing projects should be implemented by
separate organizational units, given the different nature and requirements of
these activities. BWDB has moved in that direction in 1983 when the position
of Member for OEM was established on its Board, and in 1985 when the field
organization was divided into an Implementation wing under the Member for
Implementation and an O&M wing under the Member for O&M.  The Implementation
wing was made responsible for the construction of large projects while the O&M
wing assumed responsibility for O&M of all completed projects, rehabilitation
of deteriorated schemes, and the construction of small new schemes. Given the
composition and geographical distribution of its work load and the need to
use staff resources efficiently, it would at this stage not be desirable to
establish two parallel territorial organizations, i.e. one for the
construction of all new projects (both large and small) and one for O&M of all
existing schemes. By implication, most of BWDB's field divisions will
continue to be responsible for construction, rehabilitation and O&M of series
of relatively small projects. However, in order to give due attention to O&M
and to co%.aler the natural tendency of BWDB's professional staff to favor new
construction works (in part caused by chronic underfunding of O&M activities),
the planning and budgeting process for O&M needs to be strengthened, staff
development and training activities expanded, and funding for O&M increased.
1.19       BWDB's earlier focus on construction has resulted in a
concentration of staff skills in that area. Also, training in construction
and design skills in the past was primarily made available to higher and
middle level engineering staff, largely excluding lower level staff and
beneficiaries. Consequently, BWDB staff are not adequately prepared to carry
out proper O&M. To correct this situation, training and staff development
programs must be reniirected zo provide staff witb specific O&M  skills.  The
required skills have been identified through a training need survey during
project preparation. The survey also revealed that training should be
targetted to middle and lower level staff and beneficiaries. In addition, it
highlighted the need to pay attention to staff motivation and interest in O&M
during training and the need to include training for women and the landless in
simple construction skills, as has been done in other projects.
1.20        The involvement of beneficiaries in planning, construction and O&M
of BWDB's projects is important for three main reasons, namely (a) to make the
beneficiaries partners in the development of the projects, (b) to reduce the
public burden of operating and maintaining the schemes, and (c) to encourage
the farmers to make the fullest possible use of the facilities made available
to them. With regard to the second reason, GOB recently terminated the rental
program for LLPs in BWDB's major irrigation projects and the pumps are now
being sold to the farmers, thus reducing the O&M costs of these projects. GOB



- 9 -
also is introducing new legislation to simplify the assessment and collection
procedures for water charges in BWDB's irrigation projects (para 4.13).
Concerning the third reason, there is scope in several of BWDB's projects for
farmers to cooperate with BWDB in constructing on-farm development works to
expand the area under irrig ; n.
Institutional Reviews
1.21        Several studies of BWDB's institutional capability have been made
over the last ten years by IDA staff and consultants. A major review of
BWDB's investment program and organization, undertaken jointly by GOB and IDA
staff in 1978-79, concluded that it was vital to strengthen planning in the
water resources sector. The review recommended the establishment of a Master
Planning Organization (MPO) as a separate entity. The review also led to a
follow-up study on BWDB's organizational and implementation aspects which was
undertaken in 1980-81. Some of the study's main recommendations have been
implemented over the years. These include the establishment of the position
of Member for O&M on BWDB's Board, the organizational separation of
construction and O&M, where feasible, and limited decentralization of
authority to the field. A further study of BWDB's institutional capability
was completed by consultants in 1988. The last study, together with the
studies undertaken earlier, served as a basis for an Action Plan, prepared by
IDA staff, to improve BWDB's performance. Some of the proposed actions will
be implemented shortly. These actions include (a) modernization of BWDB's
financial management and accounting systems,and (b) establishment of an
operation and maintenance cost cell. The cost cell will collect and process
accurate data on O&M costs for all projects. The data base will be used for
formulating future budget requests and for determining accurate water charges
in irrigation projects. Both actions are being su-jorted under the Second
Small Scale Flood Control, Drainage and Irrigatiou Project (Credit 1870-BD).
The present project would support actions to (a) institute a phased approach
to determine BWDB's staff requirements and training needs (paras 2.18 and
3.14), and (b) provide the Member for O&M with a headquarters techntical staff
(para 3.01). Other proposed actions are still being discussed by GOB and IDA
in the framework of proposed future lending operations.
C. Project History
1.22        The project was identified during discussions between IDA and GOB
staff in late 1982. As a first step, a pre-feasibility study was organized
with UNDP financing to assess the condition of all BWDB schemes. This study,
completed in 1984, disclosed that about one half of BWDB's 400 projects
required rehabilitation works. In the context of the proposed project,
rehabilitation was defined as the repair of facilities that have deteriorated
over time due to inadequa .e regular maintenance. It has proven useful to
distinguish two types of rehabilitation under this category, namely (a)
repairs that will restore benefits that have been lost due to deterioration,
and (b) repairs that will prevent future losses should the deterioration be
permitted to continue. The pre-feasibility study also recommended expanding
the project scope, where appropriate and feasible, to include (a) completion



- 10 -
of facilities originally planned but not carried out, (b) improvement works
that would result in greater returns than those originally planned, and (c)
additions that would extend the subprojects to areas outside the original
boundaries and/or provide a new category of benefits.
1.23         As a second step, feasibility studies were undertaken, also
financed by VNDP, for a representative cross section (by type and location) of
21 subprojects. The studies, completed in January 1989, also included the
preparation of detailed designs for the 21 subprojects and a special report on
organization and training for O&M. An IDA preparation mission recommended,
and GOB agreed, to exclude small subprojects, costing less than about
US$50,000 each, from the proposed project since the necessary rehabilitation
works could be faster implemented under other existing project arrangements.
Similrrly, it was decided to undertake the rehabilitation of sea dykes in the
coastal areas under the proposed Cyclone Protection Project, except for
emergency repair works which are being carried out under the Second and Third
Flood Rehabilitation Projects. The total number of subprojects to be
rehabilitated under this project therefore was reduced to about 80.
1.24       In addition to the proposed rehabilitation and improvement works.
separate project components were developed to improve O&M procedures and
practices in BWDB's projects and to encourage farmer participation in the
execution of on-farm development works. The BWDB Small Schemes Project (Cr.
1467-BD) already included a pilot O&M program which covered four BWDB O&M
field divisions. However, during implementation it was found that many of the
facilities under the four divisions needed major rehabilitation works prior to
the introduction of improved O&M. Consequently, the pilot O&M program could
not be implemented as intended within the time and funds available. It was
therefore decided to select a number of projects which are in reasonably good
condition and to design a program for improved O&M of those projects. A
consultancy was organized in mid-1988 to analyze O&M conditions in three
recently completed, IDA financed, major irrigation projects (i.e. Chandpur,
Karnafuli and Muhuri) and several small flood control and drainage schemes
under BWDB's Rajshahi O&M Division. This division was originally included in
the pilot O&M program of the BWDB Small Schemes Project. None of the selected
projects requires major rehabilitation works at this time but all show early
signs of deterioration due to lack of funds and poor organization of O&M. The
consultant also prepared specific proposals for two schemes to encourage
greater participation by the beneficiaries in on-farm development work for
irrigation.



- 11 -
I1. THE PROJECT
A. Rationale for IDA Involvement
2.01      During the last decade IDA has worked closely with GOB to devise
a comprehensive strategy for developing the country's water resources
and has financed a number of BWDB's flood control, drainage, and irrigation
projects (paras 1.09 and 1.10). This project forms an integral part of that
strategy. It is a national program which focusses on improving the services
provided by existing flood control, drainage, and irrigation infrastructure.
Such improvements are critical for securing the full benefits from earlier
investments. They are also critical for establishing a sound basis for the
longer term development of Bangladesh' water resources which is a pre-
requisite for continued growth of the agriculture sector. The envisaged
improvements of O&M procedures and the increased participation by the farmers
deviate from present practices and require the technical and financial backing
of an institution like IDA to succeed. Given IDA's past involvement, it has
comparative advantage to lead this initiative and to mobilize support by other
donors.
B. Project Concept and Objectives
concept
2.02      The project would support a series of interrelated actions designed
to upgrade the level of services provided by BWDB's existing irrigation, flood
control, and drainage projects. First, water control infrastructure which has
deteriorated due to lack of regular maintenance would be rehabilitated and
improved. Second, programs of improved O&M would be introduced gradually in
all projects. The program would start in selected projects which do not
require major rehabiliation works at this time, and would be expanded at a
later stage to projects where rehabilitation and improvement works have been
completed. Third, a program of on-farm development works would be carried out
in selected schemes in order to encourage farmers to make better use of the
infrastructure provided, particularly for irrigation. Fourth, BWDB's
organization for 0&M and for staff development would be strengthened, and BWDB
staff and beneficiaries would be trained, particularly for O&M tasks. Fifth,
measures would be taken to transfer part of the financial burden for OEM of
irrigation infrastructure from the public sector to the beneficiaries. Cixth,
arrangements would be made to involve disadvantaged groups such as landless
persons and women in the maintenance works of the projects.
Objectives
2.03      The project's main objectives are to protect and increase
agricultural production and incomes and to raise the standards of living,
particularly those of landless persons and women, through rehabilitation and
improved O&H of BWDB's existing projects. Several subsidiary objectives would
be pursued in order to attain the main objectives. These include (a)
increased financing and more efficient use of funds for OMH through better
planning and reduction of costs, where possible, (b) improved skills and



- 12 -
better motivation of BWDB staff for O&M tasks, and (c) increased involvement
of the farviers in planning, construction and O&M, particularly for on-farm
developmuit works.
C. Project Components
2.04      The project includes the following componentss
(a)     rehabilitation, improvement and maintenance of about 80
subprojects covering a gross area of about 600,000 ha;
(b)     improved O&M of three existing major irrigation projects
covering a net irrigable area of about 60,000 ha, and seven
small flood control and drainage subprojects covering a
gross area of about 40,000 ha;
(C)     on-farm development works in two subprojects covering about
17,000 ha;
(d)     training of BWDB staff and beneficiaries, particularly for
O&M of the subprojects;
(e)     benchmark and evaluation studies of ten selected
subprojects; and
(f)     technical assistance for planning, design and monitoring of
construction of rehabilitation and improvement works, and
for planning and implementation of improved O&M, on-farm
development works, training, and benchmark and evaluation
studies.
D. Detailed Features
Rehabilitation and Improvement Works
2.05      General.  The project would rehabilitate and improve about 80
subprojects covering a gross area of about 600,000 ha and a cultivable area of
about 400,000 ha. The subprojects are located in all parts of the country,
except the South West region where the Asian Development Bank (ADB) finances a
similar program. Subprojects would have to meet selection criteria that
emphasize their technical, environmental and economic viability in order to
qualify for financing under the project. An assurance was obtained from GOB
during negotiations that BWDB would select subprojects according to the
criteria specified in Annex 1.
2.06      Civil Works.  The civil works program would include rehabilitation
and improvement works (para 1.22). Rehabilitation works (base cost US$15.1
million) would include (a) repair, resectioning and retirement of embankments,
(b) repair of hydraulic stru.tures, including replacement of gates, repair of
protection works, and full replacement of structures where required, (c) re-
excavation of drainage canals (khals) and repair of control structures located



- 13 -
at these canals, (d) repair of revetments at embankments and river banks, and
(e) repair of operational buildings. Improvement works (base cost US$22.3
million) would include (a) new embankments and regulators in newly accreted
lands adjacent to the subprojects, (b) compartmental bunds in polders for
effective control of flooding and drainage, (c) new regulators to drainl
isolated low pockets and excavation of the associated drainage canals, (d)
additional irrigation infrastructure such as flushing sluices and irrigation
inlets at flood embankments, turnouts to field canals, and earthworks and
structures in field canals, (e) extension of river bank revetment works, and
(f) protective measures such as forestation and planting of vetiver grass
cover at embankments. Most of the earthworks for excavation of drainage
canrls and resectioning of embankments would be executed under FFW.
2.07      The design of embankments and drainage canals would conform to BWDB
standards, i.e., a 20-year return period for the embankments and release of a
ten day rainfall runoff with a return period of five and ten years for period
not exceeding three and six days, respectively, for the canals.  In earlier
IDA-financed projects, it proved difficult to enforce adequate compaction of
earthfill embankments. In accordance with the arrangements adopted for the
Third Flood Control and Drainage Project (Cr. 1591-BD) and subsequent BWDB
projects financed by IDA, consultants would monitor all construction works,
particularly compaction of embankments, to ensure quality. All contracts
would specify separate rates for (a) transporting earth from the borrow area,
placing it in layers of 15 cm (including stripping of topsoil in the
embankment base and borrow area), and turfing the embankment slopes, and (b)
breaking clods, levelling, watering if zeeded, and compacting manually.
Withdrawal applications for earthworks would be certified by BWDB confirming
that compaction was done to the specifications described in the contract
document.
2.08      Land Acquisition (base cost US$2.0 million).  About 550 ha of land
would be required, mainly for retirement of embankments in subprojects located
in the coastal belt, or bordering on major rivers where bank erosion occurs.
Land costs vary from TK 80,000 per ha in the mainland area to about Tk 250,000
per ha in some coastal polders in the South East region. Land acquisition has
been a major obstacle to the timely implementation of earlier BWDB projects
because of the lengthy administrative process and insufficient or delayed
funding by GOB. In order to avoid delays in the start-up of the project, the
first year's program would not include works requiring the acquisition of
land. With regard to land requirements for subsequent years, assurances were
obtained from GOB during negotiations that (a) processing of land acquisition
applications would start at least twelve months, (b) funds would be allocated
at least six months, and (c) BWDB would obtain possession of the land at least
one month, before construction would commence.
2.09      Maintenance of Rehabilitation and Improvement Works (Base Cost
US$10.0 million). The project would provide for maintenance of all
rehabilitation and improvement works in the subprojects starting one year
after completion of the works, i.e., when the contractor's maintenance
obligations expire. Improved maintenance procedures, as described in para
2.10 and Annex 3, would gradually be adopted in all subprojects after
completion of the rehabilitation and improvement works. The annual
maintenance cost of the completed works, on a per ha basis, would depend on
the type of subproject (flood control, drainage or irrigation) and on the



- 14 _
proportion of the subproject's facilities requiring rehabilitecion and
improvement works. The average cost is estimated at about Tk 225 (US$7) per
ha. The works would be maintained under the project for an average period of
about two years, depending on when works are completed. GOB would assume full
responsibility for providing sufficient funds to cover O&M for all subprojects
at the end of the project period. Given the persistent shortfall in O&M
funding for BVDB's projects in past years (para 1.16 and Annex 6) an
assurance was obtained from GOB during negotiations that al'ocations for
maintenance works, electricity and fuel costs in BWDB's existing Projects not
financed under the Annual Development Program would be increased by at least
1O per year in real terms from the FY89 level of TK 210 million (about US$6.5
million) until such time that all requirements are fully financed.
Improved Operation and Maintenance (Base Cost US$13.3 million)
2.10      The project would support an improved O&M program for the Karnafuli,
Muhuri and Chandpur Irrigation projects (KIP, MIP and CIP, respectively) and
seven flood control and drainage (FCD) schemes in the Nawabganj Subdivision
under BWDB's Rajshahi O&M Division. The selected projects are at present in
good physical condition and do not require rehabilitation works but current
levels of maintenance are inadequate. Therefore, to avoid deterioration and
the need for future rehabilitation, and to reduce overall maintenance costs, a
preventive and periodic maintenance program would be introduced. Preventive
maintenance would be done throughout the year and would include minor repairs
of structures, embankments and canals, including removal of water hyacinth.
Periodic maintenance works such as resectioning of embankments, re-excavation
of canals, and major repairs or replacements of structures, would follow
specific maintenance cycles, as appropriate. Earthworks Maintenance Groups
(1HG) and Structure Maintenance Groups (SMG), cno;isting of landless men and
women. would be organized to undertake the preventive maintenance program.
During recent years, maintenance work through such groups has successfully
been undertaken by the Local Government Engineering Bureau (LGEB) and other
agencies. The quality of work has proven to be better than that of
contractors, and laborers' incomes have increased considerably. Procurement
of periodic maintenance works would follow existing BWDB procedures.
Preventive maintenance and periodic structure maintenance would be undertaken
against cash payments. In CIP, preventive maintenance of earthworks would be
done under FFW on a trial basis. Special measures would be taken to ensure
that fixed minimum wages are paid to the laborers, as this practice has been
successfull since it was first applied in the Delta Development Project in
Khulna District in 1983.
2.11      Water management surveys would be undertaken in the selected
projects during the first project year to provide recommendations for
improving operations, both at the main systems and at farm level. Based on
the results of the surveys, annual iperational plans would be formulated for
the projects. These plans would emphasize improved drainage and water
distribution to ensure a dependable supply at the LLP stations and gravity
inlets. In KIP, special attention would be given to the need to use LLPs in
the area upstream of the relift pumping station. In MIP, improving the
operation of the Feni regulator would reduce the risk of downstream erosior.
and therefore the cost of maintenance. In CIP, the effectiveness of drainage
pumping would be given special attention as well as the efficiency of water
distribution in the gravity irrigated areas. In the FCD schemes under the



- 15 -
?awabganj Subdivision, the operation of sluice gates would be optimized.
2.12      Improved maintenance would be introduced during the first project
year in about 252 of the area of all selected projects. Improved operational
procedures would be adopted after completion of the operational plans. The
projects would be fully covered by the end of project year four. Improved O&M
would also be introduced from the second project year in the two subprojects
included in the on-farm development program (para 2.13).  Thereafter, the
improved system would be expanded to cover subprojects where rehabilitation
and improvement works would have been completed and the one-year maintenance
period by contractors would have expired (para 2.09). In project year four,
improved O&M would be introduced in six completed subprojects. About twenty,
ten and five subprojects would be added during project years five, six and
seven, respectively. BWDB's headquarters organization for O&M would be
strengthened to direct the design, implementation, and monitoring of long term
O&M plans for all subprojects (Chapter III). An assurance was obtained from
GOB during negotiations that improved O&M procedures would be adopted in all
operation and maintenance divisions of B3WDB in accordance with a schedule to
be submitted for review and comment to IDA by June 30, 1994. Further details
on the improved O&N program are given in Annex 2.
On-farm Development (Base Cost US$0.9 million)
2.13      The project would support an on-farm development program for the
Polder 5511 and Buri Teesta Irrigation subprojects covering a gross area of
about 17,000 ha. Rehabilitation of the main systems of the two suhprojects
would be undertaken over the first two project years and improved O&M would be
introduced from the second project year. Polder 55/1 has a large but unused
irrigatior. potential and is representative for an extensive area in the
Greater Faridpur and Patuakhali districts. The Buri Teesta Irrigation
subproject was constructed as a pilot scheme for the Teesta Irrigation Project
(500,000 ha), now under implementation, but has not yielded proper directions
for command area development and OWM. Maintenance programs, as described in
para 2.10, would be introduced in both subprojects. However, emphasis would
be placed on improved water management through increased farmer participation.
2.14      In 1-older 55/1, additional irrigation inlets would be constructed
only after Inlet Committees have been formed and beneficiaries have agreed to
make land available and to undertake all earthworks for the inlets and field
channels free of charge. Progress of this activity therefore is hard to
predict. Committees would also be formed or reactivated to manage the
existing inlets. Irrigation distribution channels would be constructed
g:zdually over a period of four years. An experimental/ demonstration program
would be undertaken which would include construction of various types of lined
and unlined field channels and buried pipe systems. Organizers/extension
workers would help form and support the Inlet Committees and would give advise
to the farmers on irrigation water management and improved cropping practices.
Water management would be improved by excavating channels for drainage and
irrigation. Drainage sluices would be supplied with sliding gates at the
country side to regulate internal water levels and Sluice Committees would be
formed to mar.age operation of the sluices. For each three to four sluices, a
sluice khalasi would be appointed. Landless cooperatives would be motivateu
to enter the water market and purchase LLPs through the Non-Government
Organization (NGO) working in the area. Credit would be sought from the



- 16 -
ongoing Dutch NGO support program or from other available sources.
2.15      In the Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject, a number of studies and
pilot activities would be undertaken to assess and improve the reliability of
water supply. Conveyance losses in the canal systems would be measured and
the need for lining examined. A water distribution program would be defined.
Ways to improve water management at farm level would be studied. A number of
alternative tertiary systems, lined and unlined, would be constructed in a
pilot area and their economic and social implications studied. New field
inlets would be constructed gradually, according to the results of the
experiments. Construction would start only after Inlet Committees have been
formed. Lined field channels would be constructed on request of Inlet
Committees only after they have agreed to donate tho land required and supply
free labor for earthworks and transporting of materials. Masons would be
provided by the project to assist the beneficiaries during construction.
Organizers/ extension workers would help form and guide Inlet Committees and
would have a supporting task in irrigation water management and agricultural
extension as well. The feasibility of dry season groundwater irrigation would
be studied and recommendations made to integrate STWs in the gravity
irrigation system. In both subprojects, agricultural extension activities
would be supported by farm demonstration plots.
2.16      The program of activities for the two subprojects would be phased as
indicated below. A broad-based baseline survey would be undertaken during the
first year to study the constraints for development and to establish a basis
for monitoring the impact of the project actions. Rehabilitation cc the main
system would be completed during the first two project years. The placement,
repair, and modification of irrigation inlets and the construction of field
channels would be undertaken during project years two through five. EMGs and
SMGs would be formed during the second project year. Inlet Committees would
be formed or re-activated during the first four project years. The
agricultural extension activities, the baseline survey, the experiments with
field distribution systems, and water management data collection would start
during the first project year. Recommendations on the introduction of STWs in
the Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject would be formulated within one year from
the start of the the project. O&H and extension activities under the on-farm
development component would continue up to year six. In project years four
and seven surveys would be undertaken to evaluate the results. Further
details are given in Annex 3.
Training and Staff Development (Base Cost US$2.7 million)
2.17      In the area of training and staff development, the project would (a)
rrovide training to upgrade the capacity of BWDB staff nd beneficiaries, (b)
strengthen personnel planning and staff development at BWDB, and (c)
strengthen BWDB's organization for training and staff development. Training
courses would be developed by consultants. Qualified trainers have been
identified among the teaching staff of universities and senior staff of BWDB.
Training materials would be adapted from relevant other projects, as far as
possible, and from overseas, if appropriate. Courses would be conducted for
about 150 engineers, 1,000 lower level staff, 900 khalasis and 2,500
repesentatives of the beneficiaries. Training for O&M would be emphasized in
the courses, especially construction quality standards and tools and materials
for the maintenance groups. Where possible, the courses would be followed by



- 17 -
annual workshops.   The project would also provide for the rehabilitation and
expansion of the Hydrological Training Institute at Bhagyakul to expand its
capacity from 25 to 50 trainees. Two mobile training vans would be provided
for skill and awareness training of maintenanc.e groups in the subprojects to
be covered by the improved O&M program.
2.18      The project would also provide expertise to develop systematic
procedures for the planning and allocation of BWDB staff resources according
to workloads, particularly for O&M. Job descriptions and work standards would
be developed for all staff positions. These, together with the annual work
programs of the divisions, would be used as a basis for drawing up staffing
plans, taking into account current staff posted in the division, their
qualifications and experience, and the possibilities of transfer,
reassignment, and retrenchment. Priority for the development of staffing
plans during the first project year would be given to the projects to be
covered by the improved O&M program and which are currently overstaffed (paras
2.10 and 3.06). In subsequent years, staffing plans would be prepared for
BWDB's other field units and its headquarters. These plans would then be
consolidated into comprehensive staff recruitment and development plans.
Computer-based data management and planning programs would be provided to
develop a staff data base. This data base would be used to analyze the impact
of various staffing patterns on recruitment, training, promotion, and other
staff development operations. Studies regarding the nature of employment
contracts and conditions, retrenchment, and st.ff mobility would be undertaken
in order to clarify issues and propose solutions to EWDB management. In order
to adequately undertake the tasks outlined abr,ve, BWDB's headquarters
organization for training and staff development would be strengthened (Chapter
III). Further details on training and staff dev,3lopment are given in Annex 4.
Benchmark and Evaluation Studies (Base Cost US$0.6 million)
2.19      Benchmark and evaluation studies would be undertaken in Buri Teesta
Irrigation, Polders 64/1A, 64/1B and 55/1, Pagner Haor Boro Protection, Sangu
River Bank Protection, CIP, MIP, KIP and one of the seven small FCD schemes in
Nawabganj O&M Subdivision. These subprojects cover the whole spectrum of
project activities. The first six subprojects would be rehabilitated and
improved. Programs for improved O&M would be taken up in CIP, HIP and KIP and
in Nawabganj O&M Subdivision, and on-farm development works would be
implemented in Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject and Polder 55/1. The studies
would covet the engineering, agricultural and socio-economic aspects with a
view of assessing the project's impact and drawing lessons during project
implementation and for similar future operations. The base line survey would
be completed during the first project year, and two impact assessment studies
would be undertaken during the fourth and seventh project years, respectively.
Further details are given in Annex 5.
Technical Assistance (Base Cost US$10.9 million)
2.20      A substantial amount of technical assistance is required to ensure
effective implementation of the project and achievement of its objectives.
The European Economic Community (EC) and the Government of the Netherlands
(GON) would employ firms of consultants to assist B1DB in Implementing the
project. Expertise would be provided to cover technical aspects such as
planning, design, construction, operation and maintenance of infrastructure,



- 18 -
and non-technical aspects including agriculture, economics, rural sociology,
environment, personnel management, training, and monitoring and evaluation.
Requirements have been estimated at 493 man-months of internationally
recruited and 1,610 man-months of locally recruited specialists. Preference
would be given to proposals with higher proportions of locally recruited
specialists. Further details on requicements and responsibilities of the
consultants are given in Annex 5. An assurance was obtained from GOB during
negotiations that all consultants would be appointed by July 31, 1990 under
terms of reference satisfactory to IDA.

E. Status of Preparation and Impl6mentation Schedule
2.22    Preparation.  Feasibility studies and detailed engineering documents
for rehabilitation and improvement works are available for 21 subprojects.
Sixteen subprojects have been retained for implementation while five
subprojects have been deleted for various reasons. The engineering documents
for the 16 subprojects were completed on the basis of surveys and
investigations conducted in 1986-87. These documents would be updated by
consultants to take into account works that have been carried out since 1987,
particularly after the floods of 1987 and 1988. The consaltants would be
recruited by EC and their work would be completed by October 1990.
Feasibility studies and detailed engineering document3 for about 65



- 19 -
subprojects would be prepared by consultants during the first four project
years. Outline programs for improved O&M, on-farm development and training
have been prepared. Detailed guidelines for the terms of reference of all
consultants, who would help direct these operations, also have been prepared
(Annex 5).
2.23    Implementation Schedule (Annex 7).  The total project implementation
period would be seven years (FY91-97). Rehabilitatioa and improvement of a
first group of 14 subprojects would start in the first project year and two
subprojects would start in the second year. Works would be completed within
three years, except for six subprojects which require further investigations
to determine effective bank protection works or organization of beneficiaries
for on-farm development works (para 2.15). These subprojects are expected to
be completed within five years. The 63 additional subprojects would be taken
up in batches of about 15-20 each after completion of the feasibili.y and
engineering documentation. All works would be completed within the project
period. Maintenance of completed subprojects would be taken up gradually as
rehabilitation works are completed, and continue until the end of the project
period. The improved O&M program would be implemented over six years (FY91-
96). During the first project year, BWDB assisted by consultants, would
prepare a detailed plan of operations for each of the subprojects to be
covered by the improved O&M program. The on-farm development program would
commence in the second project year (FY92) and would continue for a period of
five years. The training and staff development program would be implemented
over seven years (FY91-97).
F. Environmental Aspects
2.24    As most physical works consist of rehabilitation and improvement of
existing structures, embankments, and irrigation and drainage canals, the
project would improve the environmental conditions by providing added security
against flood damage, better access to properties, better drainage of areas
with excess moisture (an important public health benefit), and, for irrigation
subprojects, improved groundwater replenishment. Deepening of drainage canals
would provide for year round increased water depths in those canals and have a
positive impact on fisheries and water transportation. Some of the flood
control projects constructed earlier, were located in deeply flooded areas and
had a negative effect on the potential for fishing in the flood plains. The
present project would be free of such effects because the selected subprojects
are located, either in the coastal areas which have no flood plains, or in the
shallow to medium flooded inland areas where pre-project water body conditions
would be maintained. Migration of fish to these water bodies would continue
to be possible through effective operation of the sluices. All potential
adverse effects of the rehabilitation and improvement works wouid be assessed
during the feasibility studies to be carried out for all subprojects and
provisions would be made to remedy these effects, where necessary.
G. Project Cost
2.25    The total project cost is estimated at Tk 3,762 million (US$111.0
million), with a foreign exchange component of US$12.5 million or 11Z of the
total costs (Table 2.2). The estimates for rehabilitation and improvement
works are based on actual 1989 contract prices and the updated schedule of
rates of BWDB. Estimates of quantities are taken from the detailed



- 20 -
engineering documents for the 16 prepared subprojects. Investment costs are
expected to vary depending on the type of subproject (flood control, drainage,
irrigation) and the proportion of facilities requiring rehabilitation and
improvement. The cost of the 65 unprepared projects has been extrapolated on
a per hectare basis from the 16 prepared subprojects. The estimates for
improved O&M and on-farm development are based on a detailed analysis by BWDB
and consultants of the selected projects.
2.26    The project cost includes US$2.2 million in taxes and duties.
Physical contingencies are estimated at 1OZ of base cost of all works, goods
and services, except staff cost of BWDB. Price contingencies and foreign
exchange rates are estimated on the basis of expected rates of local and
foreign inflation as follows: local price contingencies: 102 for FY91, 8Z for
FY92 and 71 thereafter, and foreign price contingencies 7.2Z in PY91 and 4.42
thereafter.

H. Proiect Financing
2.27     The project would be financed by IDA, EC, GON and GOB.  The IDA
Credit of SDR 40.8 million (US$53.9 million equivalent) would cover about 502
of the total project cost, excluding taxes and duties. IDA would finance
investment cost for rehabilitation and improvement works and a declining
portion of BWDB's incremental O&M costs.  An EC grant of ECU 13.5 million
(US$14.9 million equivalent) would cover about 13.52 of project cost,



- 22 -
excluding taxes and duties. EC would finance over four years (MY91-94) (a)
rehabilitation and improvement of five subprojects, (b) technical assistance
for feasibility studies and designs of about 65 subprojects, (c) preventive
and periodic maintenance in the projects included in the improved O&M and on-
farm development programs, (d) training and staff development, and (e)
procurement of equipment and vehicles for use in the rehabilitation and
training programs. A grant of Dfl 25.23 million (US$12.6 million equivalent)
by GON would cover about ll.5Z of total project cost, net of taxes and duties.
GON would finance (a) technical assistance for improved OEM, on-farm
development, monitoring and quality control of rehabilitation and improvement
works, bench mark and evaluation studies, and preparation of the project
completion report, (b) a declining portion of preventive and periodic
maintenance for the improved O&M program from the fifth project year, and (c)
vehicles required for the improved OEM and on-farm development programs.
Signing of the cofinancing agreements between GOB and IC and GON,
respectively, would be a condition of effectiveness for the IDA Credit. GOB
would finance the remaining project cost, i.e., US$29.6 million. This
includes about US$20.6 million equivalent to be contributed by WFP in -the form
of foodgrains for earthworks for (a) rehabilitation and improvement works, (b)
periodic maintenance works, and (c) preventive maintenance works on a trial
basis in CIP. The balance of GOB's contribution, US$9.0 million, including
US$2.2 million taxes and duties, would be made available through annual budget
appropriations in conformance with the project implementation schedule.
Approval of the Project Proforma would be a condition of effectiveness for the
IDA Credit. Table 2.3 summarizes the project financing plan by component.
Further details are given in Annex 9.

I. Procurement
2.28     Civil Works (US$42.4 million, excluding taxes and duties).  Except
for a few major works involving river bank protection, mattressing of
embankment slopes and regulators, civil works would not be suitable for
international bidders. Most works are relatively small, labor-intensive, and
geographically dispersed in areas with difficult access. The works would
therefore be carried out under contracts let through standard local



- 23 -
competitive bidding procedures (LCB) acceptable to IDA. Contracts for
earthworks would be awarded in reaches of at least 1,000 meters; structures
would be awarded under separate contracts. GOB has discontinued the practice
of awarding contracts for structures and allowing B3DB to supply construction
materials and equipment to the contractors. An exception to this arrangement
is the fabrication of gates and lifting equipment. This work may be executed
on force account by BVDB's mechanical unit, or awarded separately under LCB
procedures. Force account work for the fabrication of gates and lifting
equipment would be limited to US$10,000 for each structure, up to an aggregate
amount of US$1.5 million. International competitive bidding (ICB) would be
used for major civil works (costing over US$500,000), including long stretches
of embankment and protection works on slopes and riverbeds which need to be
completed within one construction period, and major regulators (14 gates and
above). Bid documents for large regulators awarded under LCB procedures and
major works awarded under ICB procedures would be issued only to prequalified
bidders. Where bidders for works are prequalified, such prequalification
would be made in a manner satisfactory to IDA.
2.29     For civil works contracts estimated to cost more than US$150,000, bid
documents would be submltted to IDA for prior revipv, ar,d IDA's concurrence
would be obtained before contracts are awarded.   This procedure would apply
to about 3Z of expected contracts valued at about 40Z of the estimated total
cost of civil works. For contracts estimated to cost US$150,000 or less, bid
documents and evaluations would be retained by BVLB and made available to IDA
representatives upon request. Force account work in respect of earthworks
would be restricted to amounts under US$10,000 for each case up to an
aggregate of US$500,000.
2.30     Earthworks valued at US$20.6 million, mainly for resectioning of
embankments and re-excavation of drainage canals, would be executed under FFW
according to procedures agreed between GOB and WP. Procurement procedures
for rehabilitation and improvement works in the subprojects to be financed by
EC would be the same as for IDA-financed subprojects.
2.31     Maintenance works during construction, improved O& works, and on-
farm development works would be let under small contracts following LCB
procedures. Surveys and investigations would be undertaken by BWDB on force
account or under coutract through LCB. Bidders for survey works would be
prequalified in accordance with GOB procedures which are acceptable to IDA.
The methods of procurement for all project components are spelled out in Table
2.4.

J. Disbursements
2.32     Disbursements of the IDA Credit would extend over the period 1991-97
and would cover:
(a) 952 of expenditures on civil works, including construction materials;
(b) 10O2 of expenditures on surveys and investigations;
(c) 10OZ of BWDB's expenditures on engineering and administration at the
rate of 12Z of expenditures for civil works contracts, including



- 25 -
construction materials and earthworks undertaken with FFW under the
project;
(d)   1001 of expenditures for O&M of rehabilitated subprojects, and for the
subprojects included in the improved OEM and on-farm development
programs, until FY95; 801 of these expenditures in FY96; and 502
thereafter; and
(e) 1002 of foreign and local expenditures for training and staff
development.
2.33     Full documentazion would be sent to IDA in support of withdrawal
applications, except for items (c) and (d) above, for works executed under
force account, and for small civil works contracts individually costing less
than the equivalent of US$150,000. For these items ipplications would be
supported by statements of expenditure certified FJy .he Drector and the
Accc ntant of the Rehabilitation Directorate or OEM Directorate. The relevant
documentation would be retained by BWDB at a central location and should be
made available for review by IDA representatives on request. This
documentation also would be subject to audit. Supporting documentation for
withdrawal applications for earthworks would be certified by BWDB's Executive
Engineer confirming that compaction was done to the specifications described
in the contract document. All documents sent to IDA in support of withdrawal
applications, including statements of expenditures, would have been checked
and certified by the project consultants.
2.34     The disbursement schedule (Annex 9) reflects the anticipated sequence
of activities in implementation and has been based on the assumption that
disbursements for works done in one quarter would be completed within the next
two quarters. The disbursement profile for water development projects has
been nine years. However, in view of the simple nature of the rehabilitation
and improvement works, the advanced status of the detailed engineering
documentation and the minimal requirement of land for construction purposes, a
seven-year implementation period for the project is considered feasible.
2.35     Most of the project works would be carried out by a large number of
small contractors whose construction capability depends heavily upon the speed
at which they receive progress payments. To facilitate payments, ensure the
availability of funds, and reduce the number of withdrawal applications
submitted to IDA, GOB would open and thereafter maintain a special account in
U.S. dollars with the Bangladesh Bank in Dhaka. The authorized allocation for
the special account would be US$4.0 million equivalent. This allocation
represents an estimated four months of IDA-financed expenditures. The special
account would be replenished through reimbursements on a monthly basis and
would be used exclusively for IDA's share of financing.
K. Accounts and Audits
2.36     BWDB would maintain adequate records to identify physical progress
and financial transactions relating to the project. Individually identifiable
accounts would be kept for all expenditures for which credit withdrawals are
made on the basis of statements of expenditures (para 2.33). A separate
project account would be kept by BWDB. Auditors acceptable to IDA would audit
these accounts and statements of expenditures, where applicable. Audited



- 26 -
accounts, together with auditors' comments, would be sent to IDA within nine
months of the close uf the fiscal year. At present, BWDB's accounts are
audited by the Comptroller and Auditor-General of Bangladesh and by BWDB's
Internal Directorate of Audits. These arrangements are satisfactory to IDA.
