III. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
BWDB Headquarters Organization for Rehabilitation, Operation and Haintenance 1
3.01      In accordance vith the Action Plan to strengthen BWDB (para 1.21), a
technical unit would be established at BWDB headquarters to improve planning,
budgeting, and implementation of O&M activities. The unit would be headed by
a Chief Engineer for O&M who would be appointed to serve under the Board
Member for O&M. The unit would be composed of an O&M Directorate and a
Rehabilitation Directorate, and would carry out the folowing broad future
functions:
(a) review and consolidate the annual budget requests prepared by
the fi .d divisions which cover operation, maintenance, and
rehab .itation under different budget heads (Revenue, FFW, and
ADP) and establish priorities under budget constraints;
(b) develop long-term (five year) rolling budget requirements for
O&M covering all operational and soon-to-be completed BWDB
projects;
*c) guide the field divisions in preparing and updating O&M manuals
and prepare instructions for the operation of projects;
.d) participate in scheduled maintenance reviews and monitor the
progress of maintenance and rehabilitation works;
Ce) assist O&M field staff in solving new and difficult problems
pertaining to operation, maintenance and rehabilitation; and
(f) assist the Training Directorate (para 3.14) in preparing and
executing O&M training programs;
The unit would manage the planning and implementation of the project and
gradually assume the functions outlined above for all operational BWDB
projects. Issue of Board Orders by BWDB designating the Chief Engineer for
O&M and the Directors of O&M and Rehabilitation would be conditions of
effectiveness for the IDA Credit.
3.02      Rehabilitation Directorate.   The Rehabilitation Directorate would
be responsible for planning, designing, and monitoring of construction of all
rehabilitation and improvement works. The Directorate would be assisted by
twn teams of consultants. The first team, to be appointed by EC, would
prepare screening reports, feasibility studies, engineering documents, and O&M
plans for about 65 subprojects. Six-party meetings of representatives of MOI,
BWDB, IDA, EC, GON and WFP would be held in September and May of each year to
1 It is anticipated that institutions dealing with flood control will be
restructured under the Action Plan for Flood Control. However, the
institutional changes proposed under the present project would be
required under any restructuring plan.



- 28 -
review progress of the consultants' work. The Chief Engineer for O&H would
approve the final feasibility studies and the Director of Rehabilitction would
approve the final designs.
3.03      The second team of consultants, to be appointed by GON, would
monitor the progress and quality of rehabilitation and improvement works in
the field, check and certify applications for reimbursement to be submitted to
the donors, prepare completion reports for all subprojects, and monitor O&M of
completed works during the project period. Field staff of the consultants
would be based in four zonal offices. The consultants would develop and
operate a management information system in the office of the Director of
Rehabilitation and in the zonal offices and prepare monthly and quarterly
progress reports. In addition to information on construction progress, the
system would collect and process data on potential problems, such as land
acquisition, performance by the contractors, and issues of reimbursement. The
Director of Rehabilitation would bring persistent problems and proposed
solutions to the attention of the six-party meeting, referred to above, for
discussion and advise.
3.04      The Directorate of Rehabilitation would prepare annual work programs
and budgets for rehabilitation and improvement works, process all
reimbursement applications, guide the consultants in their work and liaise
closely with other GOB entities concerned, both inside and outside BWDB,
including local government agencies. In addition to the Director, the
Rehabilitation Directorate would be staffed with two Executive Engineers, six
Assistant Engineers, and support personnel.
3.05      Operation and Maintenance Directorate.  The OWM Directorate would be
responsible for planning, budgeting, and implementing the improved O&M and on-
farm development programs and for coordinating the project's training
activities undertaken by the Training Directorate. The O&H Directorate also
would gradually assume responsibility for the functions referred to in para
3.01 for all operational proJects. The Directorate would be assisted by a
team of consultants to be appointed by GON. This team would be headed by a
senior O&M specialist based in Dhaka, who would act as an advisor to the
Director. Other members of the team would be based in the field (para 3.10).
Assisted by the senior O&M specialist, the Directorate would (a) prepare
annual work programs and budgets for all project components, except
rehabilitation and improvement works, and monitor implementation, (b) advise
and hold regular meetings with the various field teams carrying out the
improved O&M programs, on-farm development works, benchmark and evaluation
studies, and training programs, (c) coordinate the O&M program for the entire
project, including the rehabilitated subprojects, and set up a computerized
data base to monitor preventive and periodic maintenance, (d) organize
workshops and other training related activities with the Training Directorate,
and (e) prepare and submit applications for reimbursement by the donors. The
consultant would prepare monthly and quarterly progress reports. The six
parties, referred to in para 3.01, would review the work plans, budgets, and
progress reports during their scheduled meetings.
3.06      The O&H Directorate, assisted by its consultants, also would prepare
a long-term OWM plan and an initial detailed annual O&M plan and budget for
each BWDB field division and/or project. The preparation of these plans would
start in the first project year for the projects to be covered by the improved



- 29 -
O&M program. The long-term O&M plans would, amongst others, specify all
preventive and periodic mair.tenance requirements and the staff resources
needed to efficiently operate and maintain the projects. The budgets would be
comprehensive, covering all necessary works and expenditures currently
financed separately under the kevenue Budget, FFW, and any additional funding
sources. The analysis of the four schemes carried out during project
preparation indicates that several units are currently overstaffed. The
recent transfer of LLPs to the farmers in CIP, MIP and KIP has led to a
further reduction of staff requirements. Therefore, a cr'tical review of
staff requirements in these schemes is called for in order to contain BWDB's
expenditures for O&M. Submission of a firm staffing plan, together with
proposals to transfer, retrain or retire all surplus staff currently posted in
CIP, MIP, KIP, and Nawabgani O&M Subdivision, would be a condition of
disbursement by IDA against e=penditures for operation and maintenance of all
subprojects.
3.07      During the second and subsequent project years, the O&M Directorate
would continue to prepare long-term and initial annual O0W plans and budgets.
These documenta first would cover the Buri Teesta Irrigation and Polder 5511
subprojects included in the on-farm development program; thereafter they would
cover all subprojects to be rehabilitated and improved under the project, as
well as the remaining BWDB projects and/or divisions. The second and
subsequent annual O&M plans and budgets would be prepared by the field
divisions themselves under the guidance of the O&M Directorate. From the
second project year onward, the Directorate also would prepare aggregate
annual O&M buxdgets for all BWDB projects and divisions. The accuracy of the
overall estimates would improve over time as detailed OWM plans and budgets,
referred to above, would become available for a greater number of projects and
divisions. The O&M Cost Cell, now being established under the Second Small
Scale Flood Control, Drainage and Irrigation Project (Credit 1870-BDI, would
be located in the O&M Directorate and would serve as a data base for the
preparation of O&M budgets. Assurances were obtained from GOB during
negotiations that (a) draft annual O&M programs and budgets for all
subproiects to be financed by IDA would be submitted for review and coment by
November 30,1990 for the first project year and by March 31 of each following
year, (b) finalized annual work proarams and budgets for the entire proiect
would be submitted to IDA for review by December 31,1990 for the first project
year and by July 31 of each following year, and (c) draft aggregate O&H
budgets for all operation and maintenance divisions of BWDB would be submitted
to IDA for review and comment by March 31 of each year, starting in 1992.
3.08      The O&M Directorate initially would be staffed with one Director,
one Executive Engineer, two Assistant Engineers, one Systems Analyst and
support staff. The Directorate would work closely with other BWDB units,
particularly the Directorates for Rehabilitation, Land and Water Use, and
Training. The Directorate also would coordJnate its programs with other
agencies involved in the implementation of the project, particularly BRDB,
DAE, local government entities, and NGOs.
Field Organization
3.09      Rehabilitation.  BWDB's existing field divisions, headed by
Executive Engineers, would be responsible for (a) conducting engineering
surveys and geotechnical investigations, (b) preparing bills of quantities,

estimates, land acquisition plans, and tender documents, (c) calling tenders,

Executive Engineers, through the Subdivisional Engineers and Section Officers,

implementation. The consultants regerred to in para 3.02, would monitor on-

meet the design requirements and specifications of the contract agreements.
In coordination with the O&M Directorate, the consultants also would assist

3.10      Operation and Maintenance.  BWDB's existing field divisions would
be responsible for implementing the improved O&M program starting in the first
project year in CIP, MIP, KIP and the FCD schemes under Nawabganj Subdivision.
BWDB's staff in these schemes would be adjusted according to the plan referred
to in para 3.07. BRDB would add about 20 village accountants and 100 village
inspectors to its field staff in CIP, MIP and KIP to strengthen the
organization of the village cooperatives. Through these cooperatives, BRDB
would help establish EMGs and SMGs to undertake the preventive maintenance
programs. The O&M organization in CIP, MIP and KIP would be supported full-
time during the first two project years by an internationally recruited water
management specialist, who would be based in Feni (MIP). A second
internationally recruited water management specialist would be based during
the first project year in Rajshahi to support the O&M organization for
Nawabganj Subdivision. During the entire implementation period, technical
support by locally recruited consultants and short-term internationally
recruited experts would be provided to the three irrigation projects and the
Nawabganj OM Subdivision.
3.11      On-farm Development.  BWDB's Nilphamari Water Development Division
would be responsible for implementing works in the Buri Teesta Irrigation
subproject. Staff of the division would be increased with the appointment of
one assistant irrigation engineer, one agronomist, and one socio-economist.
BRDB would intensify its activities in the subproject, emphasizing the
formation and strengthening of farmers and landless cooperatives. Its staff
at Jaldhaka Upazila would be increased by one assistant rural development
officer and three organizers to be seconded to BWDB's Nilphamari Division.
The division would be supported by a team of consultants comprising two
irrigation and drainage engineers (of which one recruited internationally for
one year), one agro-sociologist, one socio-economist, and 20
organizers/extension workers. The on-farm development program for Polder 55/1
subproject would be implemented by the Galachipa Subdivision of BWDB's
Patuakhali O&M Division. One assistant engineer, one agronomist and one
socio-economist would be added to its staff. The NGO Association for Social
Advancement (ASA) has been active in the subproject since 1987 and has helped
to organize about 50 landless cooperatives.  ASA would continue to expand its
activities and bupport the on-farm development program by (a) helping the
cooperatives register as D-Class contractors, (b) assisting the formation of
women committees, (c) identifying laborers to be included in the SMGs, and (d)
starting a program to purchase LLPs through its cooperatives. The Galachipa
Subdivision would be supported by a team of consultants with a similar
composition to that of the Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject.

and O&M.

IV. PRODUCTION, MARETING, FARM INCOMES AND COST RECOVERY
A. Agricultural Production
General
4.01       The rehabilitation works to be undertaken under the project would
either restore agricultural production to the original levels where facilities
have become non-operational due to deterioration, or secure the present level
of production against potential losses should the deterioration be allowed to
continue. Improvement works to be carried out under the project wonld result
in additional benefits. Estimates of improved agricultural production due to
the rehabilitation and improvement works have been based on a detailed
analysis of a representative sample of five subprojects examined during the
appraisal. The sample includes the Buri Teesta and Polder 55/1 subprojects
where programs for improved O&M and on-farm development would be implemented
under the project. The results of the analysis have been extrapolated to the
entire project, which would cover about 80 subprojects.
4.02       The types of rehabilitation and improvement works and associated
benefits from the appraised subprojects are as followst
(a) rehabilitation of irrigation infrastructure in the Buri Teesta
Irrigation subpruject in the North West would increase
supplementary irrigation for the aman rice and enlarge the area
cultivated with boro rice and rabi crops during the dry season;
(b) rehabilitation of embankments, improvement of drainage channels in
the Pagner Haor Boro Protection subproject in the North East would
protect maturing boro rice and rabi crops against floods during
April/May;
(c) rehabilitation of embankments, re-excavation of drainage channels
and construction of more than 100 inlets in the Polder 55/1
subproject in the South Central region would (i) protect the area
against floods, (ii) increase the area cultivated with boro rice
and rabi crops, and (iii) supply supplementary irrigation for the
aman rice; and
(d) rehabilitation of embankments and drainage channels in Polders
64/1A and 64/1B subprojects in the South East would protect the aus
and aman rice against saline water intrusion and flooding.
Crop Yields and Production
4.03       The overall increase in production from the project is expected to
be generated mainly by increased cultivation of high yielding varieties and
increase of cropping intensities. Crop yields are expected to increase
moderately with the project. Based on the projections for the sample of

B. Marketing and Prices
4.04       Bangladesh imported an average of about 1.75 million m ton food
grains per year (including food aid) between 1983 and 1988, or about 10? of
domestic consumption. Therefore, additional production of rice and wheat
under this project is not expected to cause marketing problems. Much of the
increased production will be consumed in the producing areas and surpluses can
easily be marketed through existing channels. Farmers have the option to sell
their grains under GOB's procurement program. However, since GOB procurement
prices tend to be lower than open market prices, farmers probably will sell
their produce in the local markets. The projected production increases of
vegetables, spices, oilseeds, tobacco and sugarcane are modest and farmers are
expected to sell these products locally without difficulty.
4.05       Food prices in the country are largely determined by supply and
demand. Although there are price fluctuations between locations, seasons and
years, prices in the open market generally are higher than GOB support prices.
GOB guarantees minimum prices for rice, wheat and sugarcane to protect farmers
against sharp price fluctuations and to encourage increased production of
these crops. The guaranteed prices are determined, taking into consideration
the cost of production plus a minimum of 202 markup over the cost of
production. The prices are adjusted periodically. Inputs such as
fertilizers, pesticides and irrigation equipment, were heavily subsidized by
GOB in the 19708 reaching up to 702 of the efficiency price. The policy was
reversed during the 1980s, and the subsidies on fertilizers have been reduced
every year.  In 1988, the subsidies for TSP and MP had declined to arouId 102
and 5? of their respective efficiency prices, while the price of urea exceeded
its efficiency price by about 9Z. Subsidies for irrigation equipment have
been eliminated, with the exception of DTWs for which GOB's subsidy still
represents about 66? of the efficiency price.
C. Farm Budgets
4.06       Farm budgets were prepared for each of the five appraised
subprojects under present and future with project conditions. Models were
based on owner-operated one hectare farms as about 80? of the rural households
operate farms about that size or less. The models reflect the prevailing
cropping patterns of each subproject. Gross income, cost of production, and
net income per hectare were estimated for each crop under present and with
project conditions, using actual 1989 prices. A snmmary of present, future,
and incremental farm incomes is given in Table 4.2. Detailed farm budgets are
given in Annex 11, Tables 6-10.

4.07       Rehabilitation and Improvement works in the sample of five
subprojects would increase farm incomes by 11Z to 481. Polder 6411B
subproject shows a somewhat lower projected increase of 112 because farm
incomes already are relatively high. The Buri Teesta and Polder 55,1
subprojects are expected to have the highest increase of farm incomes, 48? and
38? respectively, mainly because of additional irrigation. The distribution
pattern of additional incomes, between owner-operators, tenants and landless
laborers, is not expected to differ substantially from the present situation.
About 80Z of the land in Bangladesh is cultivated by the owners, about 152 by
tenants, and about 52 by owner-cum-tenants. Tenancy agreements are usually
based on sharecropping for one crop season only, with the landowner taking 50X
of the crop and the tenant providing all inputs. The owner-operators and
landlords would receive larger absolute gains from the project investments
than would sharecroppers and landless laborers. However, sharecroppers and
landless laborers would gain, in proportion to current income, at least as
much as owner-operators and landlords. In conclusion, the analysis indicates
that most farmers would have strong incentives to support project activities.
D. Cost Recovery
4.08       In its efforts to initiate a viable system of cost recovery for
BWDB's projects, GOB gives priority to the recovery of O&M costs of irrigation
projects because (a) costs and benefits of irrigation facilities are generally
much larger on a per ha basis than those for flood control and drainage works,
(b) irrigation benefits can more readily be quantified, and (c) beneficiaries
of irrigation are generally in a better financial position to contribute to
the projects' O&M costs. Cost recovery from the beneficiaries of flood
control and drainage works is a complex issue. GOB's ability to impose
charges is limited by a number of practical considerations. First, flood
protection in Bangladesh, as in many countries, is considered a social service
and no charges have traditionally been collected by GOB for this type of work.
Second, benefits of flood control and drainage works vary from one plot to
another, depending on their location, land elevation, and soil quality. It
would be very difficult to determine fair rates reflecting the benefits
obtained by the farmers and their ability to pay. It is therefore GOB's view,
accepted by IDA, that cost recovery for such works is not feasible at this
stage.



- ZS -
4.09       Until recently, GOB supported OLM costs of the main irrigation
systems, delivering water to the turnouts, and also part of the OM costs of
LLPs which are being used by the farmers in several of BWDB's large irrigation
projects to lift the water from the main canals to the field channels. The
LLPs were owned by BWDB and rented out to the farmers at subsidized rates.
However, the rental system was discontinued in December 1988 and the pumps
were sold to the farmers. Maintenance of the LLPs was in the past ensured by
BWDB but now is the responsibility of the farmers themselves.
4.10       Recovery of OEM costs in BWDE's main irrigation systems is governed
by the provisions of the Bangladesh Water Rate Ordinance of 1983 and the
Bangladesh Irrigation Water Rate Rules of 1984. In 1984 GOB had decide.d to set
water rates in all 12 then existing operational irrigation projects. Specific
water rates for each project and for each of the three crop seasons. based on
the estimated O&M costs of the projects, were notified by MOI in 1984.
Meanwhile, the Water Rates Rules had designated BWDB as the responsible
authority for the assessment and collection of water rates. The Rules also
authorized BWDB to designate persons, or groups of persons, other than BWDB
employees to collect water rates. Where possible, BWDB arranged for the
Krishi Samabaya Samities (KSS-village cooperative societies) to collect the
water rates.
4.11       During the first year (FY85) water rate assessments were made for
about 100,000 ha, or about 552 of the area reported to have been irrigated in
the 12 projects during that year. The balance of the irrigated area could not
be assessed due to a shortage of BDDB staff. For the same reason, assessed
areas for subsequent years have declined to a level of about 80,000 ha in
FY88. Currently, most of the projects have a backlog of assessments of two
years or more. The total assessed amount for FY85 was about Tk 34 million
(about US$ 1.1 million) of which only about 4Z has actually been collected.
Collections during subsequent years were even lower but have finally improved
in FY89 when BWDB stepped up its efforts. As of June 30, 1989 about Tk 3.5
million (about US$110,000) had been collected for FY89, or about 1OZ of the
amount assessed. There are two main reasons for the disappointing performance
of BWDB in the collection of water rates. First, the supply of irrigation
water in many of the projects is insufficient or irregular. Reliability of
water supply in those projects should be improved through rehabilitation and
upgrading of the systems before water rates can be collected effectively from
the farmers. Second, the present system of assessments and collections is
cumbersome and overly staff-intensive. It therefore has become necessary to
simplify the procedures for assessment and collection of water rates.
4.12       Over the years, IDA has been closely involved in discussions on
water rates with GOB. This is reflected in the conditionality for several IDA
financed projects of which the Second Small Scale Flood Control, Drainage and
Irrigation Project (Credit 1870-BD) is the most recent. There it was agreed
that efforts to collect water rates would be limited initially to projects, or
parts of projects, where irrigation services were found to be adequate. The
Development Credit Agreement (DCA) for Credit 1870-BD therefore specifies that
(a) water rates in selected projects with satisfactory irrigation services
would be adjusted periodically in order to reach full OH cost recovery by
June 30, 1995, (b) proposals would be prepared to improve irrigation services
in projects found to be deficient in this regard, and (c) collection
procedures would be improved in accordance with a program satisfactory to IDA.



- 39 -
4.13       After the unprecedented floods of 1987 and 1988, senior staff of
MOI and BWDB were fully occupied with arranging for repair works of damaged
projects. It became therefore necessary to delay actions on the agreements
reached under Credit 1870-BD. However, recently MOI and BWDB have prepared
preliminary proposals to improve irrigation services in deficient projects.
The proposals would be further developed and implemented under this project.
MOI also has drafted an amendment for the Irrigation Water Rate Ordinance
which incorporates two important changes designed to simplify the existing
assessment and collection procedures. First, future assessments would be made
only once every year instead of each crop season. The main irrigation season
(January through April) would be uped as the basis for setting annual water
rates covering the projects' O&M cost for tae entire year.  Second, water
rates would be assessed on a collective basis for the entire area commanded by
a LLP or a gravity outlet, instead of each individually owned parcel of
irrigated land. The owners of the LLPs and the outlet committees would be
responsible for collecting water rate payments from the farmers. These
changes would greatly reduce the cost of assessnent and collection of water
charges which is critically important for effective recovery of irrigation
costs. MOI also has drafted revised Water Rate Rules to implement the
amended Ordinance. Implementation of the revised assessment and collection
system would start in the three irrigation projects to be supported under the
project's improved O&M component, i.e., CIP, MIP, and IP. CIP was notified
in 1984 for the collection of water rates, and KIP and MHP were notified in
1989. Prcmulgation by GOB of the amendment to the Irrigation Water Rate
Ordinance, 1983, would be a condition of Credit Effectiveness. An assurance
was obtained from GOB during negotiations that the revised Irrigatlon Water
Rate Rules would be promulgated by December 31, 1990.



- 40 -
V. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. Project Benefits and Beneficiaries
5.01      The primary benefit of the project would be increased agricultural
production. Paddy production in about 80 subprojects to be implemented under
this project, would increase by about 240,000 m ton, valued at about US$37
million per year, at full development. Also, the project would save about
160,000 m ton paddy, valued at about US$25 million per year, that otherwise
would be lost due to further deterioration of project facilities. The income
from increased crop production and avoiding potential losses would directly
benefit about four million people, including sharecroppers, landless laborers
and landowners, in about 700,000 families. The project would also generate an
additional labor demand of about 25,000 man-years per year for firm
acti'ities. The additional employment created during the rehabilitation and
O&M activities is estimated at 100,000 man-years and would maitly benefit the
landless.
B. Economic Juatification
5.02      The overall economic rate of return (ERR) for the project is
estimated at 37Z. The NPV for the project is estimated at about US$79
million.  The methodology and main assumptions underlying the economic
analysis are as follows:
(a) Agricultural Development. The incremental costs and benefits, and
the ERR for the project have been based on a sample of five
representative subprojects. The key variables affecting the
viability of the subprojects are crop yields, cropping patterns, and
cropping intensities. Estimates of these are discussed in Chapter
IV and detailed in Annexes 11 and 12.
(b) Input-Output Prices. The Bank's June 1989 price projections were
used wherever applicable for traded goods. For other goods,
domestic financial prices were converted to economic prices using a
standard conversion factor (SCF) of 0.8. Unskilled labor costs were
adjusted by a conversion factor of 0.75. The financial and economic
prices are summarized in Annex 12, Table 2.
(c) Project Execution. Pre-construction activities would start in FY90.
Construction would begin in FY91 and wou.ld be completed in 'Y97.
The implementation schedule is shown in Annex 7. The period of
analysis used in calculating the ERR is 25 years.
(d) Investment Cost. Only the costs directly associated with
rehabilitation and improvement of the subprojects were counted. Of
these, transfer payments (land acquisition, taxes, and duties) and
price contingencies were excluded. Local costs were adjusted by a
SCF of 0.8.



- 41 -
te) Operation and Maintenance. Incremental costs for O&M were estimated
for each subproject and were adjusted by a SCF of 0.8.
C. Sensitivity Analysis and Risks
5.03      To test the sensitivity of the ERR to possible adverse changes in
key variables, several tests were conducted. The analysis has been based on
the representative sample of five subprojects. Table 5.1 shows the percentage
change from the appraisal values required in each of these variables to reduce
the ERR to 122 (the estimated opportunity cost of capital). The tests show
that relatively large adverse changes would be necessary to imperil the
project's economic viability.
Table 5.1: Sensitivity Analysis
Switching Value
Variable Tested                               (percentage change)
Increase of Cost of Rehabilitation
and O&M                                            +180
Reduction of Gross Crop Revenue                       -81
Increase of Cost of Farm Inputs                      +131
Reduction of Net Project Benefits                     -32
Lag in Benefits (in years)                              5
5.04      Risks.  Delays in the implementation of the project due to delays in
pre-construction activities are a risk facing the project. However, because
the project comprises a large number of subprojects, eventual delays should
affect only a limited number of subprojects. Also, the designs for the first
group of 16 subprojects to be rehabilitated during the early project years
already have been prepared and land acquisition requirements for these
subprojects are minimal. The project strategy to further reduce this risk
includes technical assistance fr planning and design of the remaining
subprojects, monitoring of rehabilitation and improvement works, and training
of BWDB staff and beneficiaries in performing their tasks. A second risk is
the possible shortage of local funds for rehabilitation and O&M of project
facilities. In order to reduce this risk the financial contribution by GOB
has been kept at a minimum during the initial post-rehabilitation years and
external donors would finance a substantial part of the incremental O&M costs
of the subprojects. Furthermoze, GOB has agreed to increase its allocations
for O&M of existing BWDB projects by 102 per year until all requirements are
fully financed (para 2.09). Special emphasis would also be given in the
project to reducing the cost and improving the standards of O&M.

VI. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
6.01 Assurances have been obtained from GOB that:
(a)   BWDB would select subprojects according to agreed criteria (para
2.05 and Annex 1);
(b)   processing of land acquisition applications would start at least
twelve months, funds would be allocated at least six months, and
BWDB would obtain possession of the land needed for construction
at least one month, before construction commences (para 2.03);
(c)   allocations for maintenance works, electricity and fuel costs in
BWDB's existing projects not financed under the Annual Development
Program would be increased by at least 1OZ per year in real terms
from the FY89 level of TK 210 million (about US$6.5 million) until
such time that all requirements are fully financed (para 2.09);
(d)   improved O&M procedures would be adopted in all BWDB operation and
maintenance divisions in at-cordance with a schedule to be
submitted to IDA by June 30, 1994 (para 2.12);
Ce) all consultants would be appointed by July 31, 1990 under terms of
reference satisfactory to IDA (para 2.20);
(f)   draft annual O&H programs and budgets for all subprojects to be
financed by IDA, draft aggregate annual O&M budgets for all BWDB
operation and maintenance divisions, and finalized annual work
programs and budgets for the entire project would be submitted for
review to IDA at specified intervals (para 3.07);
(g)   a Subproject Committee would be established before implementa-
tion of each subproject (para 3.12); and
(h)   the revised Irrigation Water Rate Rules would be promulgated by
December 31, 1990 (para 4.13).
6.02     Conditions of effectiveness for the IDA Creeit would be: (a)
effectiveness of the co-financing agreements between GOB and EC, and GOB and
GON (para 2.27); (b) approval of the Project Proforma by GOB (para 2.27); (c)
issue of Board Orders by BWDB designating the Chief Engineer for O&M and the
Directors for O&N and Rehabilitation (para 3.01); (d) issue of a Board Order
by BWDB designating the Director General for Training and Staff Development
(para 3.14); and e) promulgation by GOB of the amendment to the Irrigation
Water Rate Ordinance, 1983, (para 4.13).
6.03     . condition of disbursement against expenditures for operation and
maintenance of all subprojects would be the submission by GOB of a firm
staffing plan for BWDB's Chandpur, Muhuri, and Karnafuli Irrigation projects
and the Nawabganj OEM Subdivision (para 3.06).
6.04     With the above conditions and assurances the project would be
suitable for an IDA Credit of SDR 40.8 million (US$53.9 million equivalent) on



- 43 -
standard IDA terms with a maturity of 40 yearc. The Borrower would be the
People's Republic of Bangladesh.

BANGLADESH
BWDB SYSTEMS REHABILITATION PROJECT
Selection of Subprojects for Rehabilitation and Improvement.
Criteria for Selection of Subproiects
1.       Subprojects would be selected for rehabil'tation and improvement
among the projects listed in BWDB's inventory of operational projects. In
order to qualify for inclusion in the project portfolio proposed subprojects
must satisfy the following criteria:
(a) no other source of funding, with the exception of the Food-for-
Work program, is available for rehabilitation or improvement
works;
(b) the feasibility study of the subproject has included a review of
the technical, environmental and economic aspects of the
subproject and :onfirms that the proposed works are sound and the
subproject has a rate of return on investments of at least 15Z;
(c) the proposed improvement works are necessary; beneficiaries of
the subproject are expected to actively participate in the
subproject; and proposed solutions of possible social problems
related to implementation (such as land acquisition, beneficiary
participation in operation and maintenance, willingness to
improve cropping patterns, etc.) have been fully discussed with
residents of the subproject area, local authorities and
representatives of the technical departments concerned,
particularly agriculture and fisheries; and
(d) the total investment cost of the subproject is not less than
US$50,000 and the cost of improvements works does not exceed 501
of the total cost of the subproject.
Guidelines for Prioritizing Feasibility Studies
2.       The following guidelines should be followed to prioritize the
feasibility studies of proposed subprojects:
(a) risk of loss of benefits due to deterioration of subproject
facilities;
(b) scope for involving manual labor, particularly destitute women
and the landless, in the execution of the works;



- 45 -
ANEX 1
Page 2
(c) scope for expanding irrigation following rehabilitation of
subproject facilities;
(d) scope for minimizing possible adverse effects and increasing
benefits for special groups, particularly fishermen and boatmen;
and
(e) scope for target groups to utilize BWDB assets (embankments,
inlets, sluices) for productive purposes.

- 51 -                              ANNEX 2
Page 1
BANGLADESH
BWDB SYSTEMS REHABILITATION PROJECT
Improved Operation and Maintenance
General
1.       Oblectives.  The main objectives of the improved O&M component are to
(a) improve planning and budgeting for OEM, (b) make mox-2 efficient use of
GOB's limited financial resources for OLM by reducing the cost, where
possible, and by shifting part of the responsibilities to the beneficiaries,
(c) involve the landless, particularly women, in maintenance works, and (d)
ensure increased and sustainable agricultural production and incomes.
Selected Schemes
2.       The Karnafuli, Muhuri and Chandpur Irrigation projects and the
Nawabganj Sub-division under Rajshahi OlM Division have been selected for the
improved O&M component. The four schemes are at present in good physical
condition but insufficient maintenance is being done. To avoid deterioration
and the need for future rehabilitation, a preventive and periodic maintenance
program would be introduced.
3.       Karnafuli Irrigation Project (RIP).  The project is situated east of
Chittagong town. Two out of four irrigation units planned originally were
completed in 1984. The Halda unit comprises 19,000 ha, of which 13,000 ha is
irrigable. During high tide, water is taken into the main system from the
Karnafuli and Halda rivers by gravity. There are 15 regulators in the unit,
of which five have navigation locks. The Ichamati unit covers 3,200 ha, of
which 2,600 ha is irrigable; irrigation water is taken in from the Karnafuli
river by a main pumping plant with a capacity of 4.0 cumec, of which 1.6 cumec
is re-lifted by a second plant. In both units farmers predominantly irrigate
by LLPs.  The units have 135 km of channel and 103 km of embankment.  The
primary objective of the project is to supply irrigation water to permit
double cropping. Aman rice, which is not irrigated, is yielding about 4.0-4.5
m ton/ha, while the boro paddy yields are about 4.9 m ton/ha on average.
During 1987/88, 12,720 ha was irrigated (80 boro and 202 rabi), covering
about 90? of the command area.
4.       Muhuri Irrigation Project (MIP).  The project is located around the
confluence of the Muhuri and Feni rivers in the northeastern corner of the Bay
of Bengal. The project area is approximately 27,000 ha, of which 18,000 ha is
irrigable. The project was completed in 1985. Construction of the project
included a large regulator and a cross dam across the Feni river. This cross
dam facilitates water retention for dry season irrigation. Irrigation is
carried out by LLPs from channels and rivers. To provide better access to the
irrigation water, 175 km existing channels were improved and 20 km new
channels were excavated. A total of 1,100 pumps were planned to be fielded.
During the 1987/88 season, 963 pumps were operating, irrigating about 17,000



- 52 -                              ANNEX 2
Page 2
ha.
5.       Chandpur Irrigation Project (CIP).  The project is located on the
left bank of the Lower Meghna river, about 70 km southeast of Dhaka town. It
was constructed in the 1970s and covers a gross area of 55.000 ha. of which
about 25,000 ha are irrigable and 23,000 ha were irrigated in 1987188. A
pumping plant with six 5.5 cumec pumps provides water for irrigation and is
used for drainage as well. In almost the entire project area the water is
relifted by LLPs; only about 400 ha can be irrigated directly by gravity flow
from the canals.
6.       Nawabgani O&M Sub-division.  The Nawabganj O&M Sub-division is part
of the Rajshahi O&M Division and is responsible for seven flood control and
drainage (FCD) schemes located about 20 to 40 km northwest of Rajshahi town.
The gross area covered is about 40,000 ha.
Preventive and Periodic Maintenance Program
7*       Annual maintenance programs would be prepared which would specify
preventive and periodic maintenance works. At the core of the preventive
maintenance program is the introduction of Earthwork Maintenance Groups (EMG)
and Structure Maintenance Groups (SMG), consisting of landless women and men.
During recent years maintenance work through these groups has been
successfully done by the Local Government Engineering Bureau (LGEB) and other
agencies. The quality of the work has proven to be better and laborers
incomes have increased considerably compared with works done by contractors.
Periodic earthwork maintenance would follow BWDB's procurement procedures.
Sp-cial attention would be given to ensure that a fixed minimum wage is paid
to the laborers, as has been successfully applied since 1983 in the Delta
Development Project in Khulna District. Preventive maintenance would be done
on a continuous basis. Periodic maintenance work would follow 3- to 15-year
cycles.
8.       Preventive Earthwork Maintenance would be done on embankments and
also on canals which carry water intermittently. Organized groups of
destitute women would be employed. Destitute women are defined as female
heads of landless households who are divorced, widowed, separated or
abandoned, with dependents. Women groups are organized by a Non Government
Organization (NGO) working in the area or by the Bangladesh Rural Development
Board (BRDB) under their Mahila Bittahin Samabaya Samity (MBSS) program. The
organization of the work would be similar to the "Women Embankment Maintenance
Program" under the Delta Development Project in Khulna District. EMGs would
have a maximum of ten members, each woman initially covering about 1,000 m of
embankment or canal, depending on the need for preventive maintenance.
Maintenance would be year round, five hours a day, six days a week. Groups
would be contracted on a monthly basis. Payments would be made fortnightly
through a local bank, based on a rate in compliance with the Bangladesh Labour
Law (equivalent to 3.5 seers of paddy for an eight hour working day).
Substitutes in case of illness would be allowed and penalty clauses defined.
A Project/Polder Women Cammittee would act on behalf of the women groups
involved in maintenance and would deal with communications with the BWDB
office, recruitment of women from the various groups, fortnightly payments,



- 53 -
ANNEX 2
Page 3
and settling of penalty issues.
9.       Preventive Structure Maintenance.  A preventive structure maintenance
program similar to the Rural Employment Sector Program in Faridpur, Madaripur
and Kurigram Districts would be initiated with the assistance of a NGO or
BRDB. Only those works would be included in the program, for which the cost
of repair does not exceed 102 of replacement cost of major structures or 30Z
of minor ones. SMGs would consist of three unskilled laborers from an
organized group of landless people and would be given training as maintenance
laborers. A skilled mason or carpenter would be added to each group.
Preference in selecting craftsmen should be given to those who train the
laborers most effectively. The NGO or BRDB would advise on the selection of
the group members. Each group would be given a country boat or a van and
equipment. The group would tbe responsible for carrying materials, securing
those on site and carrying out repair and maintenance work. Materials would
be procured by BWDB on spot quotation or tendering. Work would be based on an
annual workplan but priority would be given to emergency repairs. Contracts
would be for six-month periods and penalty clauses would be defined. Payment
of the unskilled laborers would be made according to the Bangladesh Labour
Law. For the craftsmen the prevailing local rates would be followed.
10.      Periodic Earthwork Maintenance would consist of resectioning of
embankments and re-excavation of channels. The maintenance would be
implemented once per seven years, on average. For the irrigation and drainage
channels in KIP, MIP and CIP, the expected cycle is shorter -- four, three,
and five years, respectively. Work would be done under PFW or tendered
following BWDB procedures, with a contractual obligation for the contractor to
pay the laborers a minimum wage rate for a fixed amount of work. These rates
would be compatible with the FFW rates. If the contractor failed to pay the
minimum wage rate, BWDB would take action against him.  Turfing would be done
under separate contracts by womens groups.
11.      Periodic Structure Maintenance is required for major repair of
structures and buildings. The maintenance cycle for irrigation and drainage
structures and pumping stations is 15 years on average, but shorter for the
Feni regulator (ten years). Work would be tendered to contractors following
existing BWDB procedures.
Operation
12.      Based on the outcome of water management surveys to be executed in
the first project year, operation of the main system would be improved.
Annual operational plans would be formulated for the four schemes with
emphasis on improved drainage and water distribution to ensure a dependable
supply at the LLP stations and gravity inlets. In KIP special attention would
be given to the need for LLPs in the area upstream of the relift pumping
station. In MTP improved operation of the Feni regulator would reduce the
risk of downstream erosion and therefore the cost of maintenance. In CIP the
effectiveness of drainage pumping would be given special attention as well as
the water distribution efficiency in the gravity irrigated areas. In the
seven FCD schemes under the Nawabganj Sub-division the operation of sluice
gates would be optimized.



-54                                 ANNEX 2
Page 4
Timing and Coverage of Proposed Actions
13.      Improved O&M would be introduced in the first project year in about
25Z of the area of all four projects under consideration, and full coverage
would be reached by year four. The improved O&H system would be expanded to
cover other subprojects where rehabilitation is completed and the one-year
maintenance period by contractors has expired. In year four, improved O&M
would be introduced in eight additional subprojects; about 20, ten and five
subprojects would be covered during years five, six and seven, respectively.
The total area covered would be about 450,000 ha.
Responsibilities of Various Entities and Staff Requirements
14.      Assisted by a team of consultants (Annex 5), BWDB would be
responsible for implementation of the improved maintenance program.  BRDB
would intensify its activities in the four projects, emphasizing the formation
and strengthening of BSS and MBSS cooperatives which would form the EMGs and
SMGs. An analysis of the four projects indicates that the BWDB field
divisions are overstaffed. The present two divisions with seven sub-divisions
of CIP could be reduced to one division with three sub-divisions and the staff
to about 110. For MIP the project directorate with three divisions and seven
sub-divisions could be reduced to one division with two sub-divisions. The
total staff could be reduced to about 100. The set-up at KIP could remain
unchanged but the staff could be reduced to about 90. For all projects, the
number of sluice gate operators could be reduced to one per three to five
sluices. Based on the above analysis, BWDB would review its essential
personnel requirements for the four schemes and prepare a firm staffing plan,
together with proposals to transfer, retrain or retire all surplus staff
currently posted in these projects. BRDB would increase the number of its
field staff for the three irrigation projects by about 20 village accountants
and 100 village inspectors, to strengthen the organization of the
cooperatives.
Financing Arrangements
15.      In all four schemes the ongoing FFW program would be fully integrated
in the improved maintenance program. Instead of the present Project
Implementation Committees, which are established for only one construction
season, permanent Subproject Committees would be made responsible for this
program and would execute the periodic earthwork maintenance program.
Preventive maintenance as well as the periodic structure maintenance would be
done on a cash basis and would be financed up to year four by EC and
thereafter by GON on a declining basis. Earthwork preventive maintenance in
CIP would be done under FFW on a trial basis. Establishment costs, including
agreed BWDB personnel (para 14), office and transport requirements, would be
financed under the IDA Credit on a declining basis.

irrigation projects, assuming efficient BWDB staffing levels (para 14), are

BANGLADESH
BEWDB SYSTEHS REHABILITATION PROJECT
On-farm Development
General
1.       Objectives.  The on-farm development program would (a) increase the
involvement of beneficiaries and landless women and men in planning,
construction, and O&M of on-farm development work, (b) improve capabilities
and motivation of BWDB's staff for handling O&M, and (c) increase agricultural
production and incomes. Concepts of farmer participation, successfully
developed and tested by other government agencies, would be introduced.
2.       Selected Schemes.  Polder 55/1 and the Buri Teesta Irrigation sub-
project have been selected for on-farm development. Polder 55/1 has large but
unused irrigation potential, and as such is representative for an extensive
area in the Greater Faridr:r and Patuakhali Districts.  The Buri Teesta
Irrigation subproject was constructed as a pilot O&M scheme for the Teesta
Irrigation project (500,000 ha), now under implementation. The pilot scheme
still has not yielded proper directions for command area development and O&M
of the Teesta project.
Polder 55/1
3.       Background.  Polder 55/1 is one of the coastal polders in Galachipa
Upazila, Patuakhali District, about 25 km south of Patuakhali town. The polder
was completed in 1977 and its population is about 60,000. An embankment of 46
km is protecting an area of 10,800 ha against flooding. Drainage of the
polder is facilitated by nine drainage sluices. Seventeen flushing sluices
allow for irrigation by gravity; some of the sluices have a drainage function
as well. Although fresh water is available, irrigation is hardly practiced.
About 7,800 ha is cultivated, most of it single cropped. About half of the
cultivated area is under aman rice, nearly all transplanted, and about 40Z is
under boro/aus rice. Most varieties are local and yields are low (1.7 m
ton/ha on average). Rabi crops are grown on 20Z of the cultivated land. Land
distribution is very skewed, and many large landowners are absentee.
Sharecropping arrangements are extremely unfavorable for the cultivator, as
two-thirds of the crop goes to the landowner while the cultivator has to bear
the cost of all inputs. In the months March-May the polder faces some
salinity problems, although during the design stage in the 1960s rivers were
fresh year-round.
4.       retailed Objectives.  Objectives are to (a) design and implement a
proper long-term O&H program with emphasis on flood protection and tidal
irrigation, (b) reduce drainage congestion and increase the irrigation
coverage, (c) study and intensify irrigation practices, (d) increase farmers'
participation in construction and O&M, while aiming to improve the position of



- 58 -
ANNEX 3
Page 2
the women and landless, (e) strengthen agricultural extension activities, and
(f) improve the existin3 data base.
5.       Rehabilitation and Improvement.  The program comprises (a) repair and
resectioning of the embankment, (b) repair of the drainage and flushing
sluices and the installation of slide gates and modified flap gates, (c) re-
excavation of 7.5 km of drainage channels (khals) and 7.5 km of diversion
channels, (d) installation of 120 pipe inlets (25 cm diameter), and (e)
construction of 18 km of irrigation distribution channels with field turnouts,
culverts, and flumes. The works under (d) and (e) would be implemented in
phases, conditional on inputs from the beneficiaries. Tendering of earthwork
would be in accordance with BWDB procedures (para 6). Turfing would be done
by organized women groups.  BWDB staff would assist organized landless groups
to register as D-Class contractors. These groups would take part in the
tendering for earthworks on a competitive basis. Landless groups have been
organized by the NGO Association for Social Advancement (ASA), working in the
area since 1987. Altogether 50 landless cooperatives have been formed, 20
male and 30 female. Each cooperative has about 30 members.
6.       Contracting.  Earthwork for rehabilitation would be tendered
following existing BWDB procedures. BWDB would impose and enforce a
contractual obligation for the contractor to pay the laborers a minimum wage
rate for a fixed amount of work, compatible with the FFW rates.
7.       Maintenance.  Preventive earthwork and structure maintenance would be
done on a continuous base on embankments and structures, starting after
rehabilitation, and would be implemented through EMGs and SMGs (Annex 2, paras
8 and 9). The NGO ASA would be closely involved (para 12). Periodic
maintenance is required for general overhaul and major repair. For
embankments periodic maintenance would be reduced from once in four years to
once in seven years. For excavation work, the cycle would also be seven years
and for structures and buildings 15 years. Work would be tendered following
BWDB procedures. Periodic earthwork maintenance would follow the same
approach as indicated in Annex 2, para 10.
8.       Improved Water Management.  Based on the outcome of hydrological
water level and salinity measurements in the surrounding rivers, additional
pipe inlets would be provided in the embankments. Water and salinity levels
would be measured at least at five locations during the entire program, also
inside the polder, and would start as soon as possible. Data would be
correlated and analyzed. Inlets would be constructed only after Inlet
Committees had been formed and beneficiaries had agreed to make land available
and undertake the earthworks for the inlets and field channels free of charge.
Committees woule; be formed or reactivated for the 17 existing inlets.
Irrigation distribution channels would be constructed gradually over a period
of four years. An experimental/demonstration program would be launched
including construction of various types of lined and unlined channels and
buried pipe systems. The technical assistance team would include about 20
organizers/extension workers who would help form and support the Inlet
Committees. They would also urndertake extension work in irrigation water
management and agriculture. They would be guided by an irrigation engineer,



- 59 -
ANNEX 3
Page 3
an agro-sociologist and a socio-economist. Water management inside the polder
would be improved by excavation of channels for drainage improvement and
irrigation. The NGO ASA is interested in starting a program to encourage the
purchase of LLPs by landless cooperatives. In this way the cooperatives would
enter the water market. The NGO Proshika has experience in this field and is
willing to advise. Funds would be sought from the ongoing NGO-support program
under Dutch technical assistance or from other available sources. Drainage
sluices would be supplied with sliding gates at the country side to regulate
internal water levels. Sluice Committees would be formed to manage operation
of the sluices. For each three or four sluices, a sluice kalashi would be
appointed and provided with a bicycle.
9.       Agricultural Extension.  Agricultural extension activities would be
supported by the introduction of farm demonstration plots within the polder.
Each extension worker would at least supervise one plot.
10.      Monitoring.  In order to understand the lack of development and to
establish a base for monitoring, a baseline survey would be undertaken during
the first year of the project, as specified in Annex 5. Crop cuttings would
be done annually and, as a reference base, also in surrounding polders. A
data base would be set up to monitor the functioning of the Inlet Committees
and Sluice Committees. Rainfall and surface water data would be collected and
stored at the subdivisional level.
11.      Timing and Coverage of Proposed Actions.  The rehabilitation and
improvement program is planned for a period of four to five years. O&H and
extension activities under the program would continue up to year six. Repair
and modification of sluices, excavation of drainage channels, and resectioning
of the embankment would take place in the first two project years.
Construction of diversion channels, installation of inlet pipes and
construction of irrigation field channels would take place in years two
through five. EHGs and SMGs would be formed in project year two. Inlet
committees would be formed during the first three years, 130 or so in total.
They would be formed after topographic delineation of the command areas and
only after the farmers had declared their willingness to participate in
construction. The planned 120 new inlets would benefit about 1,000 ha, or 1OZ
of the subproject area. The agricultural extension activities and the
baseline survey would be taken up during the first project year. In years
four and seven follow-up surveys would be undertaken to evaluate the results.
12.       Responsibilities of Various Entities and Staff Requirements.  Work
under Patuakhali O&M Division would be redistributed so that the Galachipa
Subdivision would only be in charge of the works in Polder 55/1. The
Subdivision would have overall responsibility for the subproject. In addition
to its present positions, an assistant irrigation engineer, an agronomist, and
a socio-economist would be appointed. The staff of the Subdivision would be
supported by a team of advisers (irrigation and drainage engineers, a socio-
economist and an agro-economist) and 20 organizers/extension workers. The
tasks of the experts and the workers are described in Annex 5. The NGO ASA
would continue its present program in the area. It would support landless
cooperatives to be registered as D-Class contractors, support the formation of



- 60 -
ANNEX 3
Page 4
a Polder Women Committee, assist the Galachipa subdivision on the selection of
laborers for the SMGs, and launch a program to purchase LLPs through its
cooperatives.
Buri Teesta Irrigation Subproject
13.      Background.  The Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject is located in
Nilphamari District. The subproject was designed as a pilot scheme for the
Teesta Irrigation Project and was completed in 1967. A barrage with radial
gates has been constructed in the Buri Teesta river to store and divert water,
and a distribution system has been developed to supply water to 10,000 ha of
cultivable land. The canal system has a total length of 64 km with 262 field
outlets. Some 136 of the turnouts are connected with lined field channels.
The Buri Teesta river, a tributary of the Teesta, has been dammed at the
offtake within Indian territory. The river is now mainly fed by rainfall run-
off. Its catchment area is about 150 sq. km. The river is seasonal with
limited base flow in the dry season. The scheme is constructed mainly for
supplementary aman rice irrigation but the area actually irrigated is much
less than expected. During 1987/88, only 3,400 ha were irrigated in the aman
season and 120 ha during the rabi season. The present cropping intensity is
about 170Z. The poor performance of the scheme is due to (a) adverse physical
conditions, (b) limited water availability and poor water management, and (c)
poorly functioning agricultural extension services.   The main crops grown in
the area are b.aus and t.aman rice and jute and tobacco. BRDB is active in
the area but its program is not directed to foster farmer participation in the
scheme. No financial or labor input was given by the farmers during
construction of the field channels.
14.      Detailed Oblectives.  The subproject's objectives are (a) to design
and implement a proper long-term O&H program with emphasis on irrigation, (b)
study and improve overall and on-farm water management to increase the area
under irrigation, (c) form, strengthen, and support Inlet Committees, (d)
increase peasants' participation in construction and O&M, while aiming to
improve the position of women and the landless, (e) assess the feasibility of
ii -oducing STWs, (f) strengthen the agricultural extension activities, (g)
improve the existing data base, and (h) formulate messages for design and
operation of the Teesta Irrigation Project.
15.      Rehabilitation and Improvement.  The Teesta Main Canal is now under
construction and would cross the Buri Teesta subproject area. The area on the
left bank of the main canal would be irrigated from the Teesta. The remaining
part of the Buri Teesta scheme (6,000 ha) would remain under command of the
Buri Teesta river and would be rehabilitated under the present program. The
program includes (a) resectioning of 11 km main canal and 33 km branch canals
including shifting the embankments along the main canal, (b) modification and
repair of 91 irrigation turnouts, closing down of 45 turnouts and construction
of 50 new ones, (c) construction of 61 km of lined field channels, (d) placing
or replacing of gates in regulators and flumes for better level control, and
(e) construction of three new check structures, two escapes, five syphons for
cross-drainage and one new head regulator-cum-bridge. Lined field channels
would be constructed in phases, conditional on inputs from the farmers.



- 61 -
ANNEX 3
Page 5
Earthwork would be tendered according to current BEDB procedures.  Turfing
would be contracted to organized women groups.
16.      Malntenance.  Maintenance would be preventive and periodic, and for
both organized landless groups would be involved. BRDB has organized 15
landless men cooperatives within the project area, with about 425 members
(Bitahin Samabaya Samities-BSS), and five women cooperatives just outside the
area, with 130 members (Mahila Bittahin Samabaya Samities-MBSS). The
cooperatives are two to three years old. For the year 1989/90, BRDB plans to
organize ten BSSs and ten lBSSs within the project area. NGOs are not working
in the Buri Teesta area. MBSSs would be involved in preventive earthwork
maintenance of the canals and embankments and a Landless Women Com-ittee would
be formed (Annex 2, para 8). One woman would initially maintain 1,200 m of
embankment or 1,200 m of irrigation canal, on average. Preventive structure
maintenance would be organized as described in Annex 2, para 9. BRDB would
advise BWDB on the selection of the members for the SMGs. Periodic
maintenance would be restricted to general overhaul and major repair. For
embankments, periodic maintenance would be limited to once in five years on
average. The cycle would be 10 years for re-excavation work, and 15 years for
structures and buildings. Wcrk would be tendered following BWDB procedures.
3WDB staff would assist organized landless groups (BSS and MBSS groups) to
register as D-Class contractors for earthwork.
17.         Improved Water Management.  Water availability at the Buri Teesta
river would be assessed more accurately under the program. An automatic water
level recorder would be installed upstream in the river to measure the inflow
into the storage reservoir. The head regulators would be calibrated and their
operation recorded. Existing flumes in the system would be recalibrated, and
water levels and discharges recorded systematically. Conveyance losses in the
canal systems would be measured and the feasibility of lining assessed. A
water distribution program would be defined. Experiments would be implemented
to study water management at the farm level. A number of alternative
conveyance systems, lined and unlined, would be constructed in a pilot area
and their economic and social implications studied. Introduction of new field
inlets would be taken up gradually, taking the results of the experiments into
consideration. Inlets would only be constructed after Inlet Committees have
been formed. Lined field channels would be constructed on request of Inlet
Committees after they have shown their willingness to donate the land required
and to supply free labor for earthwork and for carrying materials. Masons, to
be provided by the project, would assist the farmers in constructing the first
150 m of lined field channel. Materials for construction of channels longer
than 150 m would be made available by the project in two installments, divided
over two construction seasons. About 20 organizers/extension workers would
form and guide Inlet Committees. They would have a supporting task in
irrigation water management and agricultural extension as well.  They would be
guided by an irrigation engineer, an agro-sociologist and a socio-economist,
and would work closely with the existing staff of the Land and Water Use
Directorate and the block supervisors of DAE as appropriate. The viability of
dry season groundwater irrigation would be studied and recommendations on
integrating STWs in the gravity irrigation system would be formulated.



- 62 -
ANNEX 3
Page 6
18.         Agricultural Extension.  The agricultural extension service under
the BWDB Land and Water Use Directorate is not functioning properly. The
number of overseers is inadequate. There are 27 block supervisors from the
DAE stationed in the area, but there is no coordination between these two
parallel organizations. Under the proposed Agricultural Support Services
project, measures would be formulated to improve the overall agricultural
exte.asion activities. In the meantime, 20 organizers/extension workers would
be provided under the technical assistance program. They would, among others,
organize and supervise the operation of farm demonstration plots within the
subproject area. Each worker would supervise at least one plot. Extension
messages would be formulated by the agronomist and agro-sociologist.
19.         Monitoring.  A baseline survey would be undertaken during the
first year of the project as a basis for monitoring. The baseline survey
would also include a number of Inlet Committees. Their functioning would be
monitored closely and progress indicators would be defined and entered into a
data base. Crop cuttings would be done annually for kharif and rabi crops, on
irrigated and non-irrigated land. A data collection and monitoring program
would be defined and implemented to record water levels and discharges in the
Buri Teesta river upstream of the weir, at the headworks and in the main
distribution systems. The functioning of various field distribution systems
would be monitored closely.
20.         Timing and Coverage of Proposed Actions.  The rehabilitation and
improvement program is planned for a period of four to five years. O&M and
extension activities would continue up to project year six. Resectioning of
the main and branch canals, replacing the gates in regulators and flumes, and
construction of new check structures, escapes, syphons, and a new head
regulator would be realized during the first two years of construction. The
placement, repair and modification of inlets and the construction of field
channels would take place during project years two through five. EMGs and
SMGs would be formed during the second year. About 140 Inlet Committees would
be formed or re-activated during the first four years. The agricultural
extension activities, the baseline survey, the experiments with field
distribution systems, and the water management data collection would be taken
up during the first vear. In years four and seven follow-up surveys would be
undertaken to evaluate the results. A recommendation on the introduction of
STWs would be formulated within one year from the start of the technical
assistance.
21.      Responsibilities of Various Entities and Staff Requirements.  The
subproject would be carried out by the Nilphamari WD Division. The Division
wrould expand its staff with one assistant irrigation engineer, one agronomist
(temporary post), and one socio-economist. Adequate support staff would be
made available for measuring water levels and discharges. The staff of the
Division would be supported by a team of advisers (two irrigation engineers,
one agro-sociologist, one socio-economist, and 20 organizers/extension
workers). The tasks and responsibilities of the experts and the extension
workers are described in Annex 5. BRDB would intensify its activities in the
area, emphasizing the formation and strengthening of BSSs and MBSS. The BRDB
staff at Jaldhaka Upazila would be increased by one Assistant Rural



- 63 -
ANNEX 3
Page 7
Development Officer (ARDO) and three organizers (two women and one man), who
would be seconded to the Nilphamari WD Division. The additional staff would
be on BWDB's payroll and would (a) support landless cooperatives to be
registered as D-Class contractors, (b) support the formation of the Landless
Women Committee, and (c) advise the WD Division on laborers to be included in
the SMGs.
Financing Arrangements
22.      Civil works for the rehabilitation and improvement program would be
financed by EC for four years, covering about 902 of the total cost of the
program. The remaining 10X would be financed after year four under the IDA
Credit. Preventive maintenance as well as periodic structure maintenance
would be on a cash basis and would be financed up to year four by EC and
thereafter by GON on a declining basis. Earthwork preventive maintenance in
Buri Teesta would be done under FFW on a trial basis. Incremental
establishment costs would be financed under the IDA Credit for the duration of
the project on a declining basis. The experimental/demonstration program
would be financed by GON.
