BANGLADESH
BWDB SYSTEMS REHABILITATION PROJECT
Training and Staff Development
A. O&M Training Program
Categories of Trainees.
1.       To increase the effectiveness of the training program, the trainees
would be grouped according to the nature of their job, their educational
background, and the type and nature of the training they would require. Five
groups of trainees are proposed, i.e., Group A: Executive Engineers (EEs),
Sub-Divisional Engineers (SDEs), and Assistant Engineers (AEs); Group B:
Sectional Officers (SOs), Surveyors (SVs), Work Assistants (WAs), Extension
Overseers/Block Supervisors (EO/BSs); Group C: Khalasis; Group D:
Beneficiaries (including Polder Committees); and, Group E: Earthwork and
Structures Maintenance Groups.
Program Content and Methods.
2.       The content of the training program would be based on the results of
the training need survey conducted in April 1988. The lists of topics given
below (para 4) were identified by BWDB managers and supervisors as areas in
which their staff would require training. Further details are available from
the data collected during the survey. The primary mode and methodology for
the courses would be a combination of discussions, demonstrations and field
assignments.
Trainers/Resource Persons.
3.       For each category of trainees and course a number of resource persons
have been identified to act as trainers. They would be provided with teaching
instructions and materials and would be given a small honorarium in addition
to fees to cover expenditures.
Course Outlines.
4.       Group A:  O&M Course and Follow-up Workshop for EEs,SDEs and AEs.
This group would attend a one-time comprehensive training course and annual
follow-up workshops. The course would be nine days long and would be
conducted in project areas like CIP, KIP, Ganges Kobadak, or Thakurgaon in
order that field visits and assignments can be included. The topics to be
covered would include: Concept and Operation of Schemes; Inspection for
Maintenance; Planning and Budgeting for O&M; Surveys and Estimates for
Maintenance; Improved Water Management Practices; Operational Hydrometry;



- 65 -
ANNEX 4
Page 2
Construction Supervision and Quality Standards; and Relations with
Beneficiaries. Participants would present papers about problems with O&M in
their zone, circle or division and discuss solutions that have been tried.
Field assignments would include inspection of earthwork and structures and the
preparation of plans to optimize maintenance procedures. The content of the
annual follow-up workshops would vary each year and focus on the issues
encountered in improving O&M that are most prevalent and pressing at that
time. The follow-up workshop may include: Review of the Topics covered in the
O&M Course; Discussion of Special Topics; Field Assignments; and, Current
Measures to Improve O&M. Senior managers and staff of BWDB, BARC, BUET, BAU,
MPO and other relevant institutions, both private and public, would be invited
to act as resource persons.
5.       Group B:  O&M Course and Follow-up Workshops for SOs,SVs, WAs and
EO/BSs.  This group would also attend a one-time training course and annual
follow-up workshops. They would attend an eight-day course at the HTI in
Bhagyakul, the IETC in Baradi or at BWDB field offices which have suitable
accomodations. Topics would cover: Concepts and Operation of Schemes;
Maintenance Inspection and Reporting; Measures and Estimates for Maintenance;
On-farm Management; Relations with Beneficiaries; and Operational Hydrometry.
Topics that are common and relevant to all the trainees in this group would be
presented in the first five days. During the remaining three days of classes
and field assignments, the topics discussed would be adjusted according to the
needs of the trainees. Each day would begin with an introduction and
discussion of the day's topic followed by field assignments for individual and
small groups of trainees. The results of the assignments would be evaluated
and discussed the following day. Trainees would have to demonstrate their
ability to organize and plan their own maintenance work and that of those they
supervise. The annual follow-up workshop would last about five days and would
address O&M problems faced by the trainee's division. The emphasis would
again be on hands-on field assignments that would cover actual measurements,
and estimating, planning, and costing of O&M work. Trainers would be selected
from the ranks of EEs and SEs of the BWDB and specialists from other projects,
including BRDB and NGO staff and local consultants.
6.       Group C:  O&M Course and Follow-up Workshop for Khalasis.  This group
would be trained in the field. A four day training course would cover: Scheme
Concept; O&M of Structures; O&M of Embankments; Maintenance Inspection and
Reporting; and Relations with Beneficiaries. Trainees would be required to
identify maintenance problems during field assignments and produce maintenance
reports and records. They would also have to demonstrate the ability to
organize beneficiaries for preventive and emergency maintenance operations.
One-day follow-up workshops would address special topics of interest to the
trainees. The trainers would be drawn from EEc, SDEs, AEs, specialists from
other projects, and representatives of NGOs and various cooperatives and
polder committees.
7.       Group D:  O&M Course and Follow-up Workshops for Beneficiaries.
This very large group of trainees would be trained in the field. The
training, utilizing the training vans, would be conducted in the villages or



- 66 -
ANNEX 4
Page 3
another suitable location where beneficiaries can be brought together.
Advanced notice of the sessions would be given through radio, BWDB staff, and
other means. Several sessions over two or three days would be held. The
training would use video demonstrations and rely on discussions to convince
beneficiaries of the benefits of proper O&M. The purpose of the training
would be to identify the role beneficiaries can play and obtain their
committment to take on a share of the responsibilities for O&M. Topics would
include: Project Concepts, Awareness and Familiarization; Operation of
Structures; Reporting Damages; Agricultural Extension; and, Leadership and
Motivation. Generally, the local SDEs, SOs, WAs and EO/BSs would attend the
course. Trainers would come from BWDB and be supplemented by local persons
from other projects. NGOs, and BRDB.
8.       Group Es  Preventive Maintenance Training for Maintenance Groups.
These groups would also be trained in the field. The training would be
conducted in the villages, at the site where the work is to be done, or other
suitable locations. The leader of the groups would be responsible for
attendance but the training sessions would also be open to those who would be
interested in joining groups or for already established groups not yet engaged
in BWDB projects. Wide advance notice of the sessions would be given through
available channels. The sessions would cover: Principles and Conditions of
Group Formation; Contract and Employment Conditions; Preventive Maintenance
Practices; Tools and Materials for Maintenance; Reporting Maintenance
Problems; and Supervision. Provisions would be made for interested persons to
register or sign-up for work. Presentors would be drawn from BWDB staff and
local representatives of NGOs, BRDB and local government.
Indicative Number of Trainees.
9.       Table 1 shows the estimated number of trainees by group who are
scheduled to attend the O&M training courses and follow-up workshops. The
trainees would be selected from BWDB staff, beneficiaries, and maintenance
groups operating in rehabilitated BWDB projects which are under O&M. The
number of maintenance groups has not been estimated at this time.
B. Strengthening BWDB's Training and Staff Development Operations.
Appointment of a Director General and two Deputy Directors.
10.      In order to strengthen the capacity and authority of the existing
Directorates responsible for training and staff development (Directorates of
Personnel; and Training, Career Planning, Operations and Methods) so that they
are able to manage the expanded training program and the staff planning and
development activities foreseen in the project, a Director General Training
and Staff Development, a Deputy Director Staff Development, and a Deputy
Director Training Evaluation would be appointed.



- 67 -
ANNEX 4
Page 4
Responsibilities, Qualifications and Experience Required.
11.      T.he Director General Training and Staff Development (DGTSD) would be
responsible for all training and manpower development activities of BVDB as
well as the related personnel operations under the Member, Administration.
For the training activities he would manage the preparation of the annual and
mid-term training plans and budgets. He would supervise the work of the
training staff including the Training Director who is responsible for
identifying the training needs of BWDB and for planning, organizing and
implementing the training programs, and the Deputy Director Training
Evaluation (new) who would be responsible for the evaluation of the impact and
cost effectiveness of all training activities. For the manpower development
activities he would manage the preparation of annual and mid-term personnel
plans and budgets. He would supervise the work of the staff of the personnel,
benefit and welfare units including the Deputy Director Staff Development
(new) who would be responsible for establishing the personnel data base and
the preparation of personnel plans including recruitment, transfers,
promotions and special analysis and studies to evaluate and improve staff
utilization in the BWDB.
12.      The DGTSD would be an experienced senior engineer with a graduate or
postgraduate degree in engineering and preferably an academic qualification in
management or economics. His experiernce should include the management of
field and headquarters departments or divisions of large public or private
sector organizations or BWDB and preferably also experience with planning and
budgeting. Experience in managing training would be an advantage.
13.      The Deputy Director Staff Development (DDSD) would be responsible for
the development of a comprehensive personnel development system. Under the
supervision of the Director Personnel he would establish a computerized
personnel data base and prepare annual and mid-term personnel plans. He would
manage analytical studies and surveys to assess the staffing patterns and
composition of headquarters and field units and determine the changing staff
requirements of these units in relation to their workload. He would prepare
proposals for the expansion and/or reduction of the staff of these units
according to actual and projected workloads, work standards, the capabilities
of staff, and personnel policies for recruitment, training, promotion,
transfers, and redundancy.
14.      The DDSD would be an experienced manpower/personnel specialist with
an undergraduate or graciate degree in management preferably with a
specialization in personnel management. Academic coursework in economics
would be an asset. He should have at least three years experience in
personnel management in a private cr public sector corporation or agency. He
should be familiar with GOB's regulations governing the employment conditions
in public sector agencies. An interest in the prevailing employment
conditions in the public and private sector would be necessary.
15.      The Deputy Director Training Evaluation (DDTE) would be responsible
for the periodic evaluation of all training activities of BWDB. Under the



- 68 -
ANNEX 4
Page 5
supervision of the Director Training, he would prepare annual and mid-term
monitoring and evaluation plans. He would design evaluation instruments for
local and foreign training programs, administer such instruments and prepare
analytical reports on the impact and cost of BWDB training. Together with the
DDSD he would determine the effect of training on the performance of the
staff. The DDTE would make recommendations for improving the cost
effectiveness of tho ongoing training programs and prepare proposals for
effective ways to improve the performance of BWDB staff.
16.      The DDTE would have an undergraduate degree in economics with a
specialization in manpower development. Experience in designing performance
evaluation instruments would be necessary. At least two years of erperience
in evaluation of training programs would be required.

BANGLADESH
BWDB SYSTEMS REHABILITATION PROJECT
Technical Assistance - Guidelines for Preparation of Terms of Reference
This Annex provides detailed guidelines for the preparation of Terms
of Reference for all consultancy services required during the implementation
of the project.  The contents of the Annex are given below.
(I)  Rehabilitation and Improvement Works
(A) Feasibility Studies and Detailed Engineering
(B) Monitoring and Quality Control
(II) Improved Operation and Maintenance
(A)  Operation and Maintenance Directorate at BWDB Headquarters
(B) Improved Operation and Maintenance Program
(III) On-farm Development Program
(IV) Training and Staff Development
(V) Benchmark and Evaluation Studies
I. REHABILITATION AND fDiWkoVEMENT WORKS
A. Feasibility Studies and Detailed Engineering
Scope of Consultancy Services
1.01     The consultancy services would cover the following:
(a) screening of about 60 subprojects (listed in Annex 1, Table 3)
and making recommendations on their eligi'ility for inclusion in
the project according to selection criteria described in Annex 1;
(b) screening of 20 additional subprojects, to be identified by BWDB,
to meet any shortfall in either the number of schemes or the area
covered;
(c) preparation of feasibility studies and detailed engineering
designs and tender documents for about 65 subprojects covering an
area of about 365,000 ha;
(d) preparation of O&M plans for all subprojects to be rehabilitated



- 71 -                         AMX 5
Page 2
(e) review of the organizational arrangements of BWDB for
implementation of the rehabilitation and improvement works and,
if applicable, recommend measures to enhance the capability of
BWDB field divisions in this respect.
Responsibilities of the Consultant
1.02     Screening of 60 Subprojects Identified by BWDB.  At the beginning of
the assignment, the consultant shall visit all identified subprojects and make
recommendations on their eligibility for inclusion in the project. The
consultants shall prepare brief field notes, summarizing their findings and
recommendations, on each proposed subproject and submit these notes, together
with the inception report, to BWDB.
1.03     Screening of 20 Additional Subproiects.  At the beginning of the
second year of his assignment the consultant shall visit 20 additional schemes
to be identified by BWDB and make recommendations on their eligibility for
inclusion. The additional schemes would be identified by BWDB within one year
after the start of the consultant's contract.
1.04     Feasibility Studies.  In order to carry out the feasibility studies,
the consultant shall:
(a) review all relevant previous engineering, agricultural and
economic studies, reports and data, including studies on any
ongoing or projected hydraulic and hydrological changes that may
affect the subprojects;
(b) determine the technical causes of under-performance and/or
problems, identify all water resources in the vicinity and assess
the potential for modifying the subproject's scope and purpose,
including irrigation and improved water management. This
assessment would be submitted to BWDB at an early stage for
review. The consultant shall then:
(i)  determine repairs, replacements and/or new structures needed
to restore and/or improve irrigation, drainage and the
effectiveness of salinity control in the subproject;
(ii)  determine retirement, realignment, raising and/or repairs of
embankments needed to restore and/or improve flood
protection; and
(iii)  check condition of installed equipment, pumps, gates,
hoists, etc.; propose repair or replacement, if necessary;
consider replacement of wooden stop-log gate systems with
steel or wooden fabricated gates and associated hoisting
arrangements; identify the causes of gate failure in the
coastal belt and mainland areas, and propose a design and an
installation method to prevent failure;
(c) identify socio-economic and other causes of under-performance
and/or problems of the subproject and consider alternative



- 72 -                         ANNEX 5
Page 3
arrangements, procedures and annual budgets needed for effective
operation and maintenance of subproject facilities.   The
consultant's Rural Sociologist shall specificallyt
(i) join the first field visits to make a first assessment of
the people's attitude towards the subproject, their opinions
about its performance in the past and scope for
improvements, and their requests for modification;
(ii) collect relevant subproject information on: population, land
distribution, development plans, and the local government
officials' opinions on the proposed rehabilitation and
improvement works; and
(iii) thereafter return to the field with socio-economic
assistants and interviewers to study in more detail: peoples
attitudes towards the subproject, opinions about its
performance in the past, suggestions for improvement,
possible conflicts of interests, requests for modification,
past and proposed future arrangements for O&M involving the
beneficiaries, and options for involving landless persons
and/or women in rehabilitation, improvement, and maintenance
works;
.d) evaluate all available data and, if needed, collect additional
data related to soils and present land use, cropping patterns,
cropping intensity, crop yields, production costs, farmers'
attitudes, etc.;
Ce) update and refine the inventory of minor irrigation equipment
operating in the subproject area, and determine the effective
area being served by that equipment;
(f) assess the possible negative effects of the subproject on boat
traffic or fisheries, and provide facilities to correct these
effects, if justified;
(g) evaluate, where applicable, the comparative effectiveness and
economies of replacing diesel pump engines with electric motors;
(h) evaluate the efficiency and productivity of current labor
intensive methods and recommend appropriate techniques for
rehabilitation and maintenance of subproject facilities; and
(i) prepare for each selected subproject a draft feasibility study,
including a preliminary design of the rehabilitation and/or
improvement works, a cost estimate and an economic evaluation.
The final report would be submitted to BWDB within two weeks
after receiving comments on the draft report.
1.05     Engineering Studies for Feasibility and Detailed Design.  For each
subproject the consultant shall undertake the following studies as appropriate
at different stages of the work:



- 73 -                         ANNEX 5
Page 4
(a) survey work:
(i)  detailed site surveys of the structures i:o be rehabilitated
or modified;
(ii) cross sections and longitudinal profiles of existing
embankments or proposed new alignments, outfall and internal
supply/drainage channels;
(iii) identification of low pockets and engineering surveys for
improved drainage;
(b) foundation and soil mechanics studies for major structures in
order to evaluate the cause of structural distress or to identify
the requirements for the foundation of new structures;
(c) hydrological assumptions, design-levels, flow capacities and
arrangements of hydraulic structures, including erosion
protection, would be reviewed and amended, if needed (with the
help of computer simulations, when necessary);
(d) study the need and economic justification for protection of
embankment slopes, especially in the coastal areas (with the help
of computer simulations when needed); low cost wave protection
measures such as vetiver grass and reafforestation would also be
considered; and
(e) study, where relevant, the piogress of erosion and/or
sedimentation along riverbanks or the sea shore using aerial
photographs and/or satellite images of different years.
1.06     Engineering Report.  After review and approval of the feasibility
report for each subproject by GOB the consultant shall prepare the detailed
engineering designs, calculations, construction drawings and cost estimates.
1.07     Tender Documents.  The consultant shall prepare for each subproject
the technical specifications consistent with BWDB standards and the
implementation schedules required for the preparation of the tender documents
by BWDB.
1.08     Operational Plan.  The consultant shall prepare a plan for operation
of the gates, pumps and other structures for each subproject. The plan would
include instructions for operation in relation to crop stages, water levels,
weather conditions and, if applicable, requirements for fisheries and boat
traffic.
1.09     Maintenance Plan.  The consultant shll prepare a maintenance plan
for each subproject.  This plan would include estimates of preventive and
periodic maintenance requirements.  The consultant shall obtain relevant
information on the experiences of the improved 0 & M component of the project
and incorporate this information in the maintenance plan of the subproject.



- 74 -                        ANNEX 5
Page 5
1.10     Final Repurt.  The final report would includes
(a) an executive summary, a scheme plan and a data sheet for each of
the 65 subprojects; and
(b) an evaluation of the System Rehabilitation Project as a whole in
sufficient detail to facilitate an investment decision by GOB and
external aid agencies on the subprojects. This would cover the
technical, financial, institutional and socio-economic aspects,
and scope and scheduling of the total project.
Duration of Consultancy Services and Composition of Team
1.11     Consultancy services would be required for a period of maximum four
years, commencing in July 1990. It is estimated that the work would require
consultancy inputs of the order of 115 and 300 manmonths of internationally
and locally recruited specialists, respectively. The consultant's team would
be headed by a Senior Water Resources Engineer and include specialists in the
following areas of expertise: planning and design, hydrology, river training,
agriculture, fisheries, soil science, environment, economics, rural sociology,
and systems analysis. The team would also be assisted by about 145 manmonths
of mid-level professionals, 2,000 manmonths of field staff (draftsmen,
enumerators, asst. surveyors, etc.) and other support staff (secretaries,
clerks, minor staff and drivers).
Reporting Requirements
1.12     The inception report would be submitted three months after the start
of the contract. It would contain the assessment of 60 identified subprojects
and recommendations on their eligibility for inclusion in the project. It
would contain a detailed wotkplan, which would be updated at regular
intervals. The consultant would submit concise monthly and quarterlv progress
reports. The final report would be submitted not later than 48 months from
the date of signing the contract.
B. Monitoring and Quality Control
Background
1.13     A total of about 80 subprojects would be rehabilitated and improved
over a period of seven years. In the first year 16 subprojects, for which
detailed documents (designs, plans, estimates and tender documents) are
available, would be taken up, followed by the remaining subprojects in batches
of about 15 each (see Annex 7).
Scope of Consultancy Services
1.14     The consultant would:
(a) monitor progress and quality of civil works construction;
(b) check and certify reimbursement claims against civil works
construction and monitor disbursements; and



- 75 -                         ANNEX 5
Page 6
(c) prepare completion reports for all subprojects and monitor
operation and maintenance of completed works during the project
period.
Responsibilities of the Consultant
1.15     Inception Phase.  The consultant would prepare a proposal for
ensuring effective supervision during implementation. The proposal would
include (a) a review of the existing capacity of the field divisions to
undertake the work load to acceptable standards and in a timely manner and
recommendations to improve the position, if required, (b) responsibilities of
BWDB's supervising staff (subdivisional engineers, section officers and work
assistants), (c) consultant's field supervision organization, and (d)
responsibilities of consultant's staff, part'>'silarly in relation to quality
control for various types of construction work, such as setting out, driving
sheet piles, erecting formwork, fixing reinforcements, concreting, backfilling
including gravel filters behind protection works, compaction at embankments,
installation of embedded parts, fixing of rubber seals in vertical and flap
gates and the installation of gates. The consultant would also prepare a
format for handing over installed gates from the contractor or BWDB's
mechanical unit to BWDB's field divisions.
1.16     Construction Phase.  The consultant would design records to be
maintained at site and monitor the keeping of such records, including all
relevant data for preparing reimbursement claims. The consultant would assist
the Director, Rehabilitation, in preparing work programs that conform to the
targets established in the project implementation schedule and the approved
Project Proforma (PP). The approved work program would also reflect the
availability of funds in the Annual Development Budget and progress in pre-
construction activities such as land acquisition, engineering surveys and
foundation investigations, detailed designs and drawings, award of contracts
and procurement of equipment and materials. The consultant would also ensure
the availability of reference points for control of alignment and levels.
qtuality control would be assured by enforcing construction procedures and
inspection standards, particularly in excavation of foundations for hydraulic
structures, concrete aggregate and concrete pours, fabrication and erection of
formwork, reinforcements, compaction at embankments, backfill at structures,
and the fabrication and installation of gates. The consultant's field staff
would supervise the work, participate in the handing over process and
countersign the final progress payment. Finally, the consultant would
identify low pockets with impeded or no drainage facilities and recommend
construction of drainage canals and/or regulators for effective drainage of
such low pockets, where needed.
1.17     Monitoring of Progress.  The consultant would establish a management
information system at each of the consultant's zonal field offices which would
be networked to the systems established at the office of the Director,
Rehabilitation. In addition to information on construction progress of each
subproject, the system would collect data on problems, particularly concerning
land acquisition, contractors' performance and progress, and issues on
reimbursements.



-76-                           ANNEX 5
Page 7
1.18     Monitoring of Quality.  The consultant would discuss the findings of
his field visits with the Executive Engineer and the Superintendent Engineer
and propose actions to be taken by them to improve progress and quality. The
field inspection notes would include observations made at the site (also
recorded in the site record book) and decisions taken by BWDB's responsible
field engineers. These notes would be distributed to the Director,
Rehabilitation.
1.19     Maintenance of Completed Subprojects.  Improved O&M procedures would
be introduced in the subprojects, starting one year after completion of
rehabilitation and improvement works. The consultant would assist the O&M
Directorate and its consultants with the preparation of preventive and
periodic maintenance plans for the subprojects and supervise implementation.
The consultant would also update the O&M manuals, prepared during the
feasibility studies, to include any changes and also prepare standing orders
for the operation of regulators in consultation with the beneficiaries.
1.20     Organization and Other Facilities.  Given the dispersion of
subprojects the consultant would set up zonal field offices in Chittagong,
Patuakhali, Bogra and Dhaka and about eight sub-offices, the location of which
would be decided during implementation. The zonal and sub-offices would be
manned by mid-level and assistant engineers, respectively. The consultant
would provide adequate office accommodation for all its staff and residential
accommodation for the internationally recruited staff. The consultant would
also provide vehicles and office equipment and arrange for its operation and
maintenance.
Duration of Consultancy Services and Composition of Team
1.21     Consultancy services would be required for a period of seven years
commencing in July 1990. It is estimated that the work would require about 68
and 200 manmonths of internationally and locally recruited specialists,
respectively. The consultant's team would be headed by a Senior Construction
Engineer and include specialists in mechanical engineering, operation and
maintenance, systems analyals and computer programming. The team would also
include about 336 manmonths of mid-level construction engineers and 672
manmonths of assistant construction engineers and other support staff
(surveyors, clerks, drivers etc.).
Reporting
1.22     The consultant would keep the Director, Rehabilitation, informed on
the progress of project implementation, including any problems which may cause
delay, and proposed solutions to these problems. In particular, the
consultant would submit:
(a) an inception report, three months after the start of the
consultancy. This report would include the proposal specified
under para 1.15, a detailed work plan indicating nature and
sequence of operations and the parties responsible for
implementation, a review of the preconstruction works completed
and any need for complementary or corrective measures, a review
of the ADP provision and work program for the first year of



- 77 -                        ANNEX 5
Page 8
implementation and how these compare with the appraisal and PP
estimates, and formats to be adopted for maintaining records at
the Eield to ensure quality and facilitate monitoring of
progress. The report would also include the methodology to be
adopted for introducing the management information system and a
timetable for implementation of the system;
(b) brief monthly and detailed quarterly reports;
(c) annual work programs to be submitted in time for the preparation
of the ADP;
(d) standing orders for operation of subprojects particularly
regulators; and
(e) proposal for construction supervision at the time of submission
of the inception report, and a proposal for maintenance at the
end of the fourth year of implementation.
II. IMPROVED OPERATION AND MAINTENANCE
A. O&M Directorate at BWDB Headquarters
Background
2.01     In 1988, an action plan to strengthen BWDB's organization and
institutional capability was prepared by IDA and discussed with GOB agencies
concerned. The. action plan consolidates major recommendations of several
studies undertaken between 1980 and 1988. It includes the recommendation to
strengthen BWDB's O&M function by providing BWDB's Member O&M with a technical
unit headed by a Chief Engineer O&M. The unit would be composed of an O&M
Directorate and a Rehabilitation Directorate. During the life of the project
the O&M Directorate would be responsible for planning, budgeting, and
implementing the im,proved O&M and on-farm development programs and for
cootdinating the ,loject's training activities (including preparation of the
reimbursement applications) to be undertaken by the Training Directorate. The
O&M Directorate would also gradually assume the functions described in para
3.01 of the main report.
Scope of Consultancy Services
2.02     The O&M Directorate, assisted by the consultant, would prepare annual
work programs and budgets for all project components, except rehabilitation
and improvement works, and monitor implementation. The consultant would also
assist the Directorate with the preparation of a long-term O&M plan and an
initial annual O&M work plan and budget for all field divisions, starting with
four projects selected for improved O&M. The consultant would design and
execute surveys of relevant conditions in the field, design detailed programs
of work, implement the programs in the field and evaluate the results. The
consultant especially would (a) advise and monitor the field teams charged
with improved OEM, on-farm development, baseline studies and training, and



- 78-                          ANNEX 5
Page 9
hold regular progress meetings with these teams, (b) coordinate the annual OEM
program of the entire project, including the rehabilitated subprojects, and
set up a computerized data base to monitor preventive and periodic
maintenance, and (c) together vith the Training Directorate, organize
workshops and other training-related activities.
Duration of Consultancy Services and Composition of Team
2.03     In order to strengthen the O&M Directorate, an internationally
recruited Senior O&M Specialist would be posted for six years, starting in
July 1990. The selected person would be the leader of the Improved OEM and
On-farm Development teams of consultants (paras 2.05 through 3.10 of this
Annex). About 12 manmonths of internationally recruited short-term specialist
services would also be provided.
Reporting Requirements
2.94     The consultant would kesp the Director O&M and the donors informed on
the progress and problems encountered and prepare:
(a) an inception report, two months after initiation;
(b) concise monthly progress reports;
(c) detailed quarterly progress reports;
-d) discussion papers, as appropriate;
'e) a draft final report, two months before the end of the contract;
and
(f) a final report at the end of the contract.
B. Improved Operation and Maintenance Program
Background
2.05     The Karnafuli, Muhuri, and Chandpur Irrigation projects (KIP, MIP and
CIP, respectively) and the Nawabganj Subdivision under Rajshahi OEM Division
have been included in the improved O&M program. The four selected schemes are
at present in good physical condition but insufficient maintenance is being
done. To avoid deterioration and the need for future rehabilitation, an
improved O&M program would be introduced. From project year four onwards the
program would be extended to other subprojects where rehabilitation and
improvement works would have been completed. Details are given in Annex 2.
Scope of Consultancy Services
2.06     In close consultation with the local BWDB staff, the consultant would
(a) review the design of the water distribution system, (b) review the water
availability and water requirements, (c) study the ongoing water management
in the distribution system and on-farm level, (d) define proposals for an
improved operation program and monitor the same, (e) develop and monitor a
long-term maintenance program, (f) increase, to the extent possible, farmers'
participation in OUM activities, and (g) set up and improve the existing data
base on OLM activities in the subprojects.



- 79 -                         ANNEX 5
Page 10
2.07     The consultant would undertake the necessary work to assess and
adjust ongoing O&H practices which would involve (a) topographic surveys and
mapping, (b) hydrology, (c) land classification and flood depth, (d) water
requirement, (e) water management, (f) socio-economic information, and (g)
agriculture and aquaculture.
2.08     To increase the area under irrigation and to improve overall water
management, the consultant would prepare and monitor operational plans based
on the analysis of historic information, and a study of ongoing irrigation
practices in a number of selected sample areas. The operation of water
distribucion at farm level would be studied, giving special attention to (a)
the relationship between water distribution, landownership, and other socio-
economic factors, (b) the efficiency of water distribution and ways to improve
it, (c) the need for further investments to optimize water distribution, (d)
farmers' participation in O&M at farm level, and (e) the functions of BWDB,
BRDB and DAE in improving water management at the farm level
2.09     To develop and implement a long-term O&M program, the consultant
would advise on the introduction of a periodic earthwork maintenance program.
To improve the quality of earthwork under this program and to enhance the
position of the laborers, the consultant would (a) advise BWDB staff on the
contracting system and monitor its implementation, (b) support the BWDB and
BRDB staff on contracting women groups for turfing the embankment, and (c)
support BRDB in enlisting BSS and MBSS cooperatives as D-class contractors.
The consultant would also ensure that the preventive maintenance program is
introduced at a rate of about 25Z of the area per year and assist BWDB with
the introduction in Chandpur of the preventive earthwork maintenance program
under FFW arrangements.
2.10     To study the viability of dry season irrigation in the FCD schemes in
the Nawabganj Subdivision, the consultant would analyze the economic
feasibility of irrigation and assess its technical and social implications.
2.11     To strengthen the agriculturallirrigation extension activities, the
consultant, in close cooperation with the staffs of BWDB's Land and Water Use
Directorate and DAE would (a) formulate extension messages and advise the
farmers accordingly, (b) advise the farmers on water management at farm level,
and (c) train extension workers.
Duration of Consultancy Services and Composition of Team
2.12     The consultancy would commence about July 1990 and would continue
till the end of June 1996.  It is estimated that 483 manmonths of specialist
input would be required, of which 87 manmontbs would be internationally
recruited specialists and 396 manmonths of locally recruited specialists.
During the first two years in KIP, HIP, CIP and the first year in Rajshahi the
O&M organization would be supported by a full-time internationally recruited
Water Management Specialist and a part-time Socio-economist, Agronomist and
O&M Engineer. From the third year local consultants in the fields of water
management, operation and maintenance, socio-economics, and women development
would take over and the internationally recruited consultants would be
employed part-time.



- 80 -                        ANNEX 5
Page 11
III. ON-FARM DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM
Background
3.01     Polder 55/1 and the Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject have been
selected for on-farm development. Polder 55/1 has large but unused irrigation
potential, and is as such representative for an extensive area in the Greater
Faridpur and Patuakhali Districts. The Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject was
constructed as a pilot scheme for the Teesta Irrigation project (500.000 ha),
now under implementation; the scheme still has to yield proper directions for
design and O&M. In Polder 55/1 and the Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject,
farmers' motivation and participation need to be enhanced. Details are given
in Annex 3.
Scope of Consultancy Services
3.02     In close consultation with local BWDB staff and the Senior
Construction Engineer in charge of monitoring the rehabilitation program, the
consultant would (a) review and adjust, if required, the plans prepared to
reduce drainage congestion and to increase the area under irrigation, (b)
study ongoing irrigation practicec and develop and test alternative irrigation
systems, (c) develop and implement a long-term O&M program, (d) increase
farmers' participation in planning, construction and O&M activities, thereby
aiming at improving the position of the women and landless, (e) strengthen the
agricultural extension activities, (f) set up and improve the existing data
base, (g) assess the feasibility of introduc!ng STWs in the Buri Teesta
Irrigation subproject, and (h) make recommendations for design and operation
for the Teesta Irrigation project.
3.03     The consultant would undertake the necessary work to asses and adjust
ongoing O&M practices as described in para 2.07.
3.04     To improve the quality of earthwork under theR rehabilitation and
improvement program and to enhance the position of the laborers, the
consultant would (a) scpport the NGO ASA work.'ng in Polder 55/1 and BRDB in
the Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject in enlisting labor groups as D-class
contractors, (b) advise BVDB staff on the contracting system, fixing the
conditions of paying a minimum rate to the laborers, and monitor the
implementation, (c) support the BWDB staff, the NGO in Polder 55/1, and BRDB
in the Buri Teesta Irrigation subproject in contracting women groups for
turfing of embankment.
3.05     To develop and test alternative irrigation systems, the consultant
would (a) assess ongoing irrigation practices in the polder and their
coverage, (b) design and implement, on a pilot basis, field conveyance systems
(lined and unlined, surface and buried), monitor their technical and socio-
economic performance, and (c) define an on-farm water distribution system.
3.06     To increase the area under irrigation and to improve the overall
water managem6s.., the consultant would (a) in close consultation with the
farmers, verify the locations where new irrigation inlets have been planned
and assess the feasibility of lining, (b) form Inlet Committees and monitor
their functioning; the Inlet Committees should be a fair representation of the

command area and would be formed only after the beneficiaries have agreed to

for one year in the Burn Teesta Irrigation subproject. Subsequently, local
consultants in the fields of irrigation and drainage, and agro-sociology would
take over, supported by short-term visits of the internationally recruited
specialists.

IV. TRAINING AND STAFF DEVELOPMENT
Background
4.01     Since its creation, BWDB has undertaken a program of training to
upgrade and prepare its staff for the different jobs in the organization.
This training has focused on the administrative and engineering skills of the
higher and middle level staff.  With the increased responsibility for O&M and
rehabilitation there is a need to refocus training on the skills required for
these activities. Also, because of the nature of O&M and rehabilitation,
which depends critically on the involvement of middle-and lower-level staff as
well as beneficiaries, the training program would need to be extended to these
groups. Finally, in view of the high cost of training and the use of training
for staff motivation, upgrading and promotion, it is necessary to undertake
regular evaluations of the impact of the training on the performance of staff.
4.02     Assistance would be provided under the project to strengthen BWDB's
Training and Staff Development functions. A Director General (DG) would be
appointed to manage the expanded training and staff development activities.
He would supervise the existing Directorates of P,irsonnel, and Training and
Career Planning, the Engineering Academy in Kaptai, the Hydrology Training
Institute in Bhagyakul, and the Irrigation Extension Training Centre in
Baradi. He would assure that the new activities are coordinated with the work
of other Directorates that deal with staff matters. In addition, a Deputy
Director (DD) for Staff Development and a Deputy Director for Training
Evaluation would be added to the Directorates of Personnel, and Training and
Career Planning, respectively, to help implement the improved training and
staff development activities. The DG would be responsible for the following
new tasks:
(a) developing comprehensive staffing plans showing the optimum
allocation of all categories of staff to headquarters, and zonal,
circle and divisional field units in relation to the workload of
these units;
(b) undertaking the necessary analysis to design measures to deal
with overstaffing and surplus categories of staff including
improved procedtres for promotion (based on performance),
rotation, transfer and retrenchment (to improve staff morale);
(c) designing and conducting new training programs for BWDB staff,
beneficiaries. and the maintenance groups in OSM and
rehabilitation; and
(d) undertaking, for the first time, evaluations of the ongoing and
new training programs to determine their benefits in terms of
improved performance of staff in relation to the cost of the
training.



- 83 -                         ANNEX 5
Page 14
Technical Assistance Requirements
4.03         To assist the B8WDB with these activities, many of which are
complex and new, the following consultant services would be provided:
(a) Internationally Recruited Specialists. Three internationally
recruited specialists would be provided, i.e. (i) a personnel
management and training specialist for two years to assist the
Director General with the execution of his new functions and
responsibilities, (ii) a personnel specialist for one year to
assist the Director Personnel and the new Deputy Director Staff
Development, and (iii) an evaluation specialist for two four-
month visits to assist the Director Training and Career
Development and the new Deputy Director Training Evaluation.
Total requirements of internationally recruited specialists are
estimated at 44 manmonths.
(b) Locally Recruited Specialists. A team of locally recruited
specialists would be provided to (i) assist BWDB in staff
planning, (ii) produce the training materials during the first
project year, (iii) org.nize and conduct the training courses
during the second and third years of the project, and (iv) design
and undertake the evaluation of existing and new training
courses. Total requirements of locally recruited specialists is
estimated at 150 manmonths.
(c) Study Tours. The consultants would organize study tours to give
the Director General, the Directors of Personnel, and Training
and Career Development, and the two new Deputy Directors (Staff
Development and Training Evaluation) an opportunity to study and
discuss training and staff development programs and issues in
other countries. Two study tours would be organized, i.e. (i) a
three-week study tour to visit water resources management
agencies in Europe and the US, and (ii) a two-week study tour in
the region to study personnel management, for instance at the
International Irrigation Management Institute in Sri Lanka or the
Irrigation and Water Management Ministry in the Philippines.
Execution
4.04     The personnel management and training specialist and the personnel
specialist would be required from the beginning of the project to assist BWDB
with the development of the staffing plans for the four schemes selected for
improved OWM. They would also assist with the development of staff
utilization standards (e.g. miles of embankment per khalasi) and the
improvement of existing job descriptions or the development of new ones. The
specialists would design the methodology for the quantification of divisional
work programs and staffing requirements on the basis of the staff utilization
standards and job descriptions in order to arrive at optimum staff



- 84 -
ANNEX 5
Page 15
utilization. They would compare existing staff allocations with optimum ones
and undertake socio-economic and employment studies to determine what can be
done to eliminate staff imbalances. The methodology developed for optimum
staff utilization would eventually be used to prepare staff plans for all BWDB
divisions. In undertaking this work the specialists would work closely with
the BWDB staff and utilize the assistance of local specialists, if necessary.
4.05     The personnel management and training specialist would also assist
the Director of Training and the Director6 of the (aptai Engineering Academy,
and Bhagyakul Hydrology Training Institute with the design and implementation
of all training programs. In addition, he would manage the loral specialists
who would design and develop the new O&M training programs for BWDB staff and
beneficiaries and the Rehabilitation training program for staff and the
preventive maintenance groups.
4.06      The training programs would be based on similar programs which have
been developed in other projects (e.g. the Pukka Maintenance Groups of the
Rural Employment Sector Program in Faridpur, Madripur, and Kurigram Districts)
and overseas. Qualified teaching staff would be drawn from among the BWDB
Staff, research institutes, universities and consultarxts. In principle each
category of trainees would participate in an O&M/Rehabilitation course and, if
possible, in an annual follow-up workshop. Appropriate :earning materials,
including picture-based materials for illiterate trainees and video-based
materials for field training of groups would be developed for the programs.
The training would emphasize practical, hands-on experience (e.g. hydrological
measurements, interviewing farmers, forming groups, calculating sections,
repairing gates, etc.) for all types of trainees. Two mobile training units,
equipped with audio and video, would be provided by the project.
4.07         The training evaluation specialist would assist the Director of
Training with the development of course and program evaluation instruments and
the methodology to conduct a periodic cost-benefit analysis of the training.
The focus of the evaluations would be the assessment of the relevance or
usefulness of the training as experienced by the trainee, the impact of the
training on the performance of the trainee as experienced by his supervisors,
and the cost of the training in relation to other alternatives. The
specialist is expected to assist also with the development of general
performance evaluation systems which would be used in conjunction with the
staff plans, job descriptions, promotions and retrenchments, discussed above.
Reporting Requirements.
4.08     The consultant would prepare and submit the following reports: (a) an
inception report after two months, detailing the consultant's proposed work
program, (b) a report, after eight months, on the methodology and issues of
developing work programs, work standards and staff assignments and the
application of this methodology to the staffing of the four schemes selected
for improved OEM, (c) a study after 16 months of staff transfer, rotation,
retirement and other methods of staff development and retrenchment in the
context of BWDE employment conditions and contracts, (d) detailed plans after

five months for the production of training materials, their verification and

performance evaluation, and (g) monthly and quarterly progress reports.
V. BENCHMARK AND EVALUATION STUDIES
Background
5.01.    Bencbmark and evaluation studies would be undertaken in the
following subprojects: Buri Teesta Irrigation, Polders 64/1A, 64/1B, and 55/1,
Pagner Haor Boro Protection, Sangu River Bank Protection, KIP, MIP, CIP, and
one of the seven FCD schemes in Nawabganj 0 & M Subdivision. The first six
subprojects would be rehabilitated during the first one to three years and
programs of improved 0 & M would be introduced thereafter. Programs for
improved 0 & M would be taken up from the fist project year in KIP, MIP, CIP,
and in Nawabganj 0 & M Subdivision. A program of on-farm development works
would be implemented in Buri Teesta and Polder 5511. The studies would cover
the engineering, agricultural, and socio-economic aspects with a view to
assessing the project's impact and drawing lessons for similar future
operations. The baseline survey would be completed during the first project
year and two impact assessment studies in the fourth and seventh project
years, respectively.
Benchmark Studies
5.02     Benchmark studies would be conducted with the objective of
providing or verifying data on engineering, agricultural and socio-economic
aspects prevailing prior to the rehabilitation and/or the introduction of
improved 0 & M in the subprojects. The engineering data would include
details on inventory and the operational condition of existing flood control,
drainage and irrigation facilities (including privately owned irrigation
equipment), the incidence and impact of flooding in the subprojects and
adjacent areas, and the extent of past relief efforts, including Food-for-Work
(FFW) programs, for rehabilitation works following floods. An assessment
would be made of the potential for future development, particularly of
irrigation, through modification or expansion of existing facilities. The
agricultural and socio-economic data would cover cropping patterns,
cultivation practices, use of inputs, crop yields and production, livestock
and fisheries activities, land distribution and fragmentation, land tenure
system, cost of production, income levels for various groups of project
beneficiaries (including landless laborers), marketing channels, formal and
informal groups of farmers and their activities, and other local institutions
serving agriculture and their effectiveness. Detailed information would be
collected or verified on agricultural losses during the last five years due to
malfunctioning of existing infrastructure or exceptional floods. Possible
underperformance of existing irrigation facilities and the technical and/or



- 86 -
ANNEX 5
Page 17
socio-economic causes thereof would also be investigated during the study.
Priorities for development, as perceived by the farmers, would also be
ascertained. A substantial part of the information would be available in the
project preparation documents, while the balance would have to be collected
through interviews with farmers and landless persons (both men and women),
local leaders, and representatives of support agencies.
Evaluation Studies
5.03     Evaluation studies would be undertaken in order to assess the
effectiveness of project activities and determine their impact on agricultural
production, farm incomes, and farm level employment for different categories
of people (landowners, sharecroppers, landless laborers) in the subprojects.
To that end all relevant information collected during the benchmark surveys
would be updated using the same samples, to the extent possible. The studies
would specificallyt
(a) identify technical causes of any under-performance of the
subprojects related to inadequacies in planning, design,
construction, operation and maintenance and recommend
rectifications and/or additional works to improve efficiency;
(b) identify any organizational or budgetary constraints and
management problems with 0 & M and other causes of under-
performance of the subprojects, and suggest alternative
arrangements, procedures, and financial provisions for effective
0 & M;
(c) identify the potential for further development, particularly of
irrigation, and propose appropriate modifications of scope and
purpose of the subprojects;
(d) examine the contributions of various support services, i.e.,
agricultural extension, credit institutions, input supply
agencies, and recommend measures to improve their effectiveness;
(e) examine the farmers' involvement and participation in the use and
maintenance of project facilities, particularly with regard to
the program of on-farm development works, and recommend measures
to increase their involvement, if necessary; and
(f) examine changes in the positions of landless laborers and women
and recomnend actions to improve their situations.
Execution
5.04     The procedures for conducting the benchmark and evaluation studies
would be similar. Most of the information required to assess the subprojects'
engineering aspects would be on file at BIWDB (feasibility reports, engineering



- 87 -
ANNEX 5
Page 18
documentation, 0 & M manuals). The engineer of the study team would
complement the available information by his own observations and through
discussions with BWDB staff concerned and the farmers. Most of the
information on agricultural and socio-economic aspects would have to be
collected through field surveys.
5.05      It is envisaged that the field surveys would be undertaken in two
phases. During the first phase the study team would collect relevant
information from statistical sources and agricultural support services
operating in the subproject areas. The study team would also conduct group
interviews with village leaders, farmers and landless people in a
geographically well distributed and representative sample of about 25
villages. Separate interviews would be held with groups of women. In small
subprojects with fewer than 25 villages, group interviews would be held in all
villages. During the benchmark studies the respondents would be asked to
discuss their expectations of the subproject, both positive and negative.
During the evaluation studies an assessment would be made of changes due to
the subproject, both qualitative and, to the extent possible, quantitative.
5.06     During the second phase, detailed interviews would be held with about
150 farm households in three villages in each subproject. In each survey
village a stratified random sample of about 50 farm households would be
selected on the basis of farm size and land ownership status. Factual
information would be collected on all relevant aspects which should be
sufficient to prepare typical pre-project (benchmark studies) and post-project
(evaluation studies) farm budgets and to estimate the economic rate of return
on rehabilitation and improvement works. The farmers' opinions would be
sought on subjects such as (a) planned or actual changes in cropping patterns
and practices due to the subproject, (b) any problems due to the subproject or
not covered under the subproject and measures taken or proposed to solve these
problems, (c) assistance received from GOB support agencies, and (d)
participation in 0 & M and on-farm development works and suggestions for
improving existing or proposed arrangements. About 201 of the respondents
should be women (to be interviewed by female surveyors) in order to assess
changes in their position due to the subproject.
Schedule of Surveys and Composition of Study Teams
5.07     The consultanting firm, to be recruited internationally for the
improved 0 & M and on-farm development program; would also be responsible for
undertaking the benchmark and evaluation studies. An internationally
recruited evaluation specialist would be provided for a total of 12 manmonths,
of which four months during the benchmark studies and eight months during the
two evaluation studies. The assignment would be for two months at the
beginning of each study to organize the work programs and two months at the
end of the studies to help consolidate the study team's findings and
recommendations in concise draft reports. The surveys would be undertaken by
a team of locally recruited experts (u.4iversity staff and students or
cnsultants) under a subcontract with the internationally recruited consulting
firm.  The studies would be undexi-ken cver twelve month periods during



- 88 -
ANNEX ;
Page 19
project years one, four, and seven. Given the number of subprojects to be
studied it is proposed to divide the local consultant team into two or three
sub-teams. Total requirements for the three studies are estimated at 288
manmonths local professional staff in the following discipliness socio-
economics, agricultural economics, engineerivg, agronomy, operation and
maintenance, and rural sociology (male and female). The team would also
require support staff, including surveyors (male and female), statistical
officers, enumerators, data processing officers, and field supervisors.  The
consultans would provide their own transport, office accommodation, equipment,
and report printing and reproduction.
Reporting
5.08     The consultant woull submit the following reports:
(a) an inception report within two months after the start of the
studies; the report would include comments on the terms of
reference, work plan, method of execution including the names of
all staff to be deployed, etc;
(b) brief monthly progress reports and detailed quarterly progress
reports within two weeks of the end of the reporting period;
(c) one draft final report for each of the benchmark and evaluation
studies which would summarize the main findings and
recommendations for all subprojects, one month before the end of
the studies;
(d) one final report for each of the studies at the end of the
assignment; and
(e) lists of surveyed farms with location and addresses for possible
reidentification for subsequent studies, questionnaires and work
files, with all relevant subproject information collected during
the studies, together with the final reports.

BANGLADESH
BWDB SYSTEMS REHABILITATION PROJECT
Operation and Maintenance Cost Estimates and Budget Allocations
Background
1.       At present BWDB is responsible for O&M of over 400 completed
projects, ranging in size from small single structure schemes to large
multipurpose projects. Infrastructure in these projects includes, among
others, about 6,850 km of embankments, 4,900 km of irrigation canals, 6,500 km
of drainage channels, 60 km of revetments, 6,000 sluices, 140 cross dams, 80
groynes, 850 bridges, 225 km of roads and 1,350 buildings and sheds. The main
sources of funding for O&M of these assets are GOB's Revenue Budget and the
Food-for-Work Programme (FFW). BWDB uses up to 90Z of its FFW allocation for
repair and maintenance of embankments and channels in completed projects and
the balance for construction of earthworks in new schemes. O&M of completed
parts of new projects is usually financed from BWDB's Development Budget until
the projects are fully completed.
2.       Funding requirements for O&M activities in operational projects are
estimated each year by BWDB's field staff. The estimates are prepared
separately for esach of the budgets mentioned above and are submitted by the
field offices to successive higher levels of BWDB's management structure for
review and approval. However, at no point during the review process are the
different requests combined into one document showing total O&M costs for all
BWDB projects separately and together. Instead, the separate budget requests
are used as a basis for separate consultations between BWDB management and the
Treasury and BWDB management and the World Food Programme (WFP).
Subsequently, the Treasury and WFP determine their allocations for BWDB as a
whole for the next financial year. BWDB management then distributes the
budget allocations over the various field offices and projects, subject to
approval by MOI.
3.       The process summarized above has several disadvantages.  First,
insufficient guidance is being given to BWDB field staff to ensure consistency
in the preparation of O&M cost estimates. Also, since actual budget
allocations tend to fall far short of requirements, field staff lack the
incentive to prepare accurate estimates. Second, lack of comprehensive and
accurate estimates for each project hampers decision making on funding of O&M
costs and on the recovery of O&M costs from the beneficiaries in BWDB's
irrigation projects. Third, the estimates are limited to the next financial
year only although sound financial planning would require the preparation of
tentative O&M cost estimates for at least the next five years. And, fourth,
BWDB does not r-ztinely update future O&M cost estimates for projects still
under construction. In order to overcome these difficulties it was decided to
establish an O&M Co'st Cell under the Second Small Scale Flood Control,
Drainage and Irrigation Project (Credit 1870-ED) with technical and financial



- 90 -
ANNEX 6
Page 2
support by the Canadian International Development Agency (CIDA). The Cost
Cell would create and maintain a cowputerized register of up-to-date OWM cost
estimates for all new and completed projects. Pending the establishment of
the Cost Cell, two sources of information on OWM costs are available, i.e.
BWDB's own estimates with the limitations discussed above and estimates
prepared by Engineering and Planning Consultants (EPC) Ltd. during the
feasibility studies for this project.
O&M Cost Estimates

FY89 and FY90 are summarized below (in Tk million):

For the purpose of estimating BNDB's O&M cost, debt service payments should
not be included since BVDB does not generate income to cover the payments.
Similarly, incidental expenditures for items such as the Joint River
Commission and taxes should be excluded from the O&M cost estimates.
5.       BWDB's allocation under the FFW Programme for FY89 was 97,500 m ton
of wheat with a value of about Tk 536 million. It is estimated that about 90?
of the allocation is being used for repair and maintenance work in operational
projects, corresponding to about Tk 482 million. Although BWDB's original
request exceeded the final allocation by a factor of about four because of the
large backlog of maintenance works and the flood damage incurred in 1988, it
is assumed that the final allocation reflects approximately the annual
requirements in normal years.
6.       BWDB's estimates suggest that total requirements for OWM of its
completed projects in FY89 were on the order of about Tk 1,220 million (about
US$ 38 million).

irrigation projects indicates a relatively large surplus of BWDB staff even
before the rental program was terminated. The total O&M cost of all
operational projects is therefore rrobably somewhat lower than estimated by
the consultants. For the purpose vf this analysis it is estimated at US$ 40
million for FY89.
